صاحب
فيهما واحد إذ لا عبرة بكون الأولى نافذة أو غير نافذة لامتناع مرور أهلها في الثانية مطلقاً فأطلقه المؤلف فشمل النافذة وغير النافذة، وقيد المتشعبة بكونها غير نافذة لأنها لو كانت نافذة لساغ للعامة المرور فيها فلا يمتنع فتح باب لأهل الأولى بها. وتقييد صاحب الهداية تبعاً للفقهيين وقع اتفاقاً ولذا صورها كثير من أهل التحرير نافذة وكثير غير نافذة وأما المتشعبة عنها فأجمعوا على تصويرها غير نافذة فتأمل ذلك تفهمه .اهـ. وسيأتي ما فيه قوله : فالذي يمكنه أن يفتح باباً في الزائغة القصوى الخ) المراد بالإمكان التصور لا الجواز يعني أن الذي يتصور له فتح باب في الزائغة المتشعبة هو الدار التي في ركن المتشعبة لأن جداره فيها أما من قبله فلا يمكنه ذلك لأن جداره في الأولى. وإنما فسرناه بذلك لأنه لا يجوز له فتح الباب فيها كما ذكره المؤلف قوله : (وإنما قلنا ليس له ذلك لأن فتحه للمرور الخ) قال الرملي وذكر في جامع الفصولين عن شيخ الإسلام أن له الفتح والمرور ثم قال : في المسألة اختلاف الروايات واختلاف المشايخ، واختار شيخ الإسلام أن له ذلك مطلقاً . و به يفتى ثم رمز (الض) وجعله خلاف ظاهر الرواية وأقول : وعلى ظاهر الرواية مشت المتون والله تعالى
Book of Hawalah, Chapter of Arbitration. The other builds: the owner of the upper floor has the right to its stability, and the owner of the lower floor has the right to repel rain and sun from the lower. So ownership is absolute but the right is a restriction, and they have combined, so we combine them. The completion is in it. And regarding a wall between two: if they have wood upon it and one of them builds, the builder may prevent the other from placing wood until he gives him half the value of the building as built. In al-Aqdiya: a shared wall, one wants to demolish it and the partner refuses: if it is in a condition not feared to collapse, he is not compelled; if it is feared to collapse, according to Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, he is compelled. If they demolish it and one wants to rebuild and the other refuses: if the foundation of the wall is wide enough that he can build a wall in his share after division, the partner is not compelled; if it is not possible, he is compelled. So from Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, and upon it is the fatwa. The interpretation of compulsion is that if the partner does not agree, he spends on construction and then recovers from the partner half of what he spent. In Shihadat al-Fadli: if they demolish it and one refrains, he is compelled; if it collapses, he is not compelled, but he is prevented from using it until he recovers half of what he spent, if that was done by the order of the judge; if without order, then half the value of the building. So in Fath al-Qadir. In Jami' al-Fusulain: if the owner of the lower demolishes his lower and the owner of the upper his upper, the owner of the lower takes his lower? Since he has caused loss of a right attached to ownership, he guarantees just as if he caused loss of ownership. End quote. Its apparent meaning is that there is no compulsion on the owner of the upper, and what is in Fath al-Qadir is contrary, and the second is apparent. The first is interpreted as when the owner of the lower builds his lower and asks the owner of the upper to build his upper, then he is compelled. If the lower collapses without action of its owner, he is not compelled to build due to lack of transgression; the owner of the upper may build if he wishes, then build his upper upon it, then seek recourse and prevent him from dwelling until he pays him, because he is compelled, like a borrower of a pledge who pays the debt without permission of the pledgor is not a volunteer. If the upper and lower both collapse, the same applies. Then recourse for the value of the building or what he spent: it is said if the owner of the upper is compelled, he recovers from the owner of the lower the value of the lower as built, not what he spent; it is said if he built by judge's order, he recovers what he spent, otherwise he recovers the value of the building, and with this fatwa is given. So in Qismat al-Walwaliya. The permission of the partner is like the permission of the judge, so he recovers what he spent as verified by Allamah Ibn al-Shihnah in Sharh al-Manzumah. If we say he recovers the value of the building when there is no permission, then is the value considered at the time of building or at the time of recourse? Two opinions, and the correct is... His statement: (and the second is apparent) he meant what is in Jami' al-Fusulain because he mentioned it after the previous statement of al-Fath. And his statement: 'and the first is interpreted as when he built, etc.' he meant by the first what is in al-Fath from his statement: 'if they demolish it and one refrains, he is compelled'. This interpretation contradicts what he previously presented from al-Dhakhirah: that the ceiling of the lower, its beams, its rafters, its planks, and its mud belong to the owner of the lower, and accordingly the owner of the upper is not compelled to build therein because there is no harm to the owner of the lower in leaving it; rather there is benefit of lightening his ceiling. Reflect. Then it appeared to me that there is no contradiction between what is in al-Fath and what is in Jami' al-Fusulain, because what is in al-Fath concerns the shared wall, and what is in Jami' concerns the lower and upper, and the difference is more apparent than that... about it similar, not through, the inhabitants of the first may not open a door in it, unlike the circular. He claimed a house in the hand of the time of building, and it is built on the basis that the builder builds on the property of the partner or on the property of the builder then transfers from it as well. In Jami' al-Fusulain: a wall between them, and each has a load on it, so the wall weakened; one wanted to raise it to repair it and the other refused. The one wanting repair should say to the other: raise your load with pillars and supports, and inform him that he wants to raise it at such-and-such time, and have witness on that. If he does so, otherwise he has the right to raise the wall; if his load falls, he does not guarantee. End quote. His statement: (a crooked street, elongated, branching from it a similar one not through, the inhabitants of the first may not open a door in it, unlike the circular) i.e., an alley as in al-Mi'raj; Taj al-Shari'ah interpreted it as a dead-end alley, named so for its deviation from the main road; in Ghayat al-Bayan it is interpreted as a neighborhood, named so for its inclination from side to side, from 'zaghat al-shams' when it inclined. In al-Tahdhib: al-za'ighah is the road that turns away from the main, and al-mustatilah is the long one from istital meaning became long. The author did not explicitly restrict the first to being non-through, following most books; al-Hidayah restricted it following al-Faqih Abu al-Layth and al-Tamartashi, and the author's statement can be understood from his saying 'similar to it, non-through' so he made the second like the first with the restriction of non-through. The shape of the long one is: O (road) O O O O O. Then the one who can open a door in the farthest crooked street is the owner of the house that is in the corner of the second crooked street. We only say he does not have that because opening it is for passage, and the inhabitants of the first crooked street have no right of passage in the second. His statement: (and the author did not explicitly restrict the first to being non-through, etc.) al-Ramli said: the apparent is that the ruling for both is the same, since there is no consideration of the first being through or not through because passage of its inhabitants in the second is absolutely prevented, so the author left it general, including the through and non-through. He restricted the branching one to being non-through because if it were through, the public could pass through it, so opening a door for the inhabitants of the first would not be prevented. The restriction by the author of al-Hidayah following the two jurists happened coincidentally, so many of the investigators depicted it as through and many as non-through. As for the one branching from it, they all agreed on depicting it as non-through. Reflect to understand. End quote. There will come what is in it. His statement: (So the one who can open a door in the farthest crooked street, etc.) what is meant by possibility is conceptual, not permissibility, meaning the one who can conceive of opening a door in the branching crooked street is the house that is in the corner of the branching, because its wall is in it; as for the one before him, he cannot because his wall is in the first. We only interpreted it that way because he is not permitted to open the door in it as mentioned by the author. His statement: (and we only say he does not have that because opening it is for passage, etc.) al-Ramli said: and in Jami' al-Fusulain from Shaykh al-Islam that he has the right to open and pass, then he said: there is difference in reports and difference among shaykhs, and Shaykh al-Islam chose that he has that absolutely, and with this fatwa is given. Then he symbolized (ض) and made it contrary to the apparent narration, and I say: on the apparent narration the texts rely, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings from the Hanafi school on two distinct topics: (1) rights and obligations regarding shared walls and upper/lower floors, and (2) the rules of opening doors in dead-end streets (za'igha). The text begins with a fundamental principle: the owner of an upper floor has the right to its stability, while the lower floor owner has the right to protect from rain and sun. Both ownership and the right coexist, so the law reconciles them. Then it discusses a shared wall: if one partner builds, he can prevent the other from adding wood until compensated. Regarding demolition of a shared wall, if it is safe, a partner is not forced to rebuild; if it is dangerous, he is forced. If both demolish and one wants to rebuild, he may be forced if the foundation can only support a single wall after division; otherwise not. The 'compulsion' means he can build and recover half the cost from the partner. If a building collapses without fault, no one is forced to rebuild, but the upper owner may build and then reclaim costs from the lower owner, preventing him from using the building until paid. The amount recoverable depends on whether the judge authorized the construction or not. The text then resolves an apparent discrepancy between Fath al-Qadir and Jami' al-Fusulain by distinguishing between a shared wall (where compulsion applies) and separate lower/upper floors (where it does not). The second part discusses the za'igha: a dead-end alley that branches off from a main road. Residents of the first alley cannot open a door into the branching alley because they have no right of passage there. Only the owner of the house at the corner of the branching alley may open a door. There is a difference of opinion regarding whether this is absolute or if the first alley residents can also open doors; the relied-upon fatwa (according to Shaykh al-Islam) allows it absolutely, but the majority of texts follow the apparent narration that prohibits it.

Key Terms
الجبر (al-jabr): compulsion: a court order forcing a co-owner to participate in rebuilding or allowing the other to build and recover costs.
السفل (al-sufl): the lower floor or ground floor of a building.
العلو (al-'uluww): the upper floor or story of a building.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a shared wall collapses without the fault of either owner, what is the ruling?
A) Both are forced to rebuild.
B) Neither is forced to rebuild.
C) Only the owner of the lower floor is forced.
D) The judge may order rebuilding at his discretion.

Key Takeaway
Compulsion to rebuild a shared structure depends on fault, danger, and the possibility of separate construction; natural collapse does not trigger compulsion.

Rulings on Dead-End Streets (Za'igha)
Rulings on dead-end streets (za'igha).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a za'igha (dead-end alley) and state the Hanafi ruling on opening doors into branching alleys, distinguishing between the majority opinion and the fatwa of Shaykh al-Islam.

Core Idea
Residents of a dead-end alley cannot open a door into a branching dead-end alley because they have no right of passage there.

This section of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses the rights of residents in dead-end streets (za'igha). It defines the za'igha as a crooked alley that does not lead through, and rules that inhabitants of the first alley cannot open a door into a branching alley. There is a difference of opinion, with some scholars allowing it absolutely. The passage clarifies the majority view and the relied-upon fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Define the za'igha
Learn the linguistic and legal definition: a crooked, dead-end street that branches off a main road.
Synonyms: سكة غير نافذة, محلة.
2
Identify the two types
The text distinguishes between a first alley (الأولى) and a second branching alley (المتشعبة). The second is explicitly non-through; the first is not restricted in the text but some scholars restrict it.
3
State the ruling
Residents of the first alley may not open a door into the second branching alley because they have no right of passage there.
4
Recognize the exception
Only the owner of the house at the corner of the branching alley can open a door into it, since his wall is in that alley.
5
Understand the difference of opinion
Shaykh al-Islam allowed residents of the first alley to open a door and pass, and this is the fatwa according to some. But the majority of texts hold the prohibiting view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة باب التحكيم
يبني الآخر لذي العلو حق قراره ولذي السفل حق دفع المطر والشمس عن السفل فالملك مطلق والحق مانع وقد اجتمعا فجمعنا بينهما وتمامه فيه. وفي الحائط بين اثنين لو كان لهما عليه خشب فبنى أحدهما للباني أن يمنع الآخر من وضع الخشب حتى يعطيه نصف قيمة البناء مبنياً. وفي الأقضية : حائط مشترك أراد أحدهما نقضه وأبى الشريك إن كان بحال لا يخاف سقوطه لا يجبر، وإن كان بحيث يخاف عن الإمام أبي بكر محمد بن الفضل يجبر . وإن هدماه وأراد أحدهما البناء وأبى الآخر إن كان أساس الحائط عريضاً يمكنه أن حائطاً في نصيبه بعد القسمة لا يجبر الشريك، وإن كان لا يمكن يجبر ؛ كذا عن الإمام أبي بكر محمد بن الفضل وعليه الفتوى. وتفسير الجبر أنه إن لم يوافقه الشريك أنفق على العمارة ورجع على الشريك بنصف ما أنفق . وفي شهادات الفضلي : لو هدماه وامتنع أحدهما يجبر ولو انهدم لا يجبر ولكن يمنع من الانتفاع به ما لم يستوف نصف ما أنفق فيه إن فعل ذلك بقضاء القاضي وإن كان بلا قضاء فبنصف قيمة البناء ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وفي جامع الفصولين: لو هدم ذو السفل سفله وذو العلو علوه أأخذ ذو السفل سفله إذ فوت عليه حقاً ألحق بالملك فيضمن كما لو فوت عليه ملكاً ا هـ . وظاهره أنه لا جبر على ذي العلو وظاهر ما في فتح القدير خلافه والظاهر الثاني، ويحمل الأوّل على ما إذا بنى صاحب السفل سفله وطلب من ذي العلو بناء علوه فإنه يجبر . ولو انهدم السفل بغير صنع صاحبه لا يجبر على البناء لعدم التعدي ولصاحب العلو أن يبني إن شاء ويبني عليه علوه ثم يرجع ويمنعه من السكنى حتى يدفع إليه لكونه مضطراً كمستعير الرهن إذا قضى الدين بغير إذن الراهن لا يكون متبرعاً، ولو انهدم العلو والسفل فكذلك ثم الرجوع بقيمة البناء أو بما أنفق قيل إن كان صاحب العلو مضطراً يرجع على صاحب السفل بقيمة السفل مبنياً لا بما أنفق، وقيل إن بني بأمر القاضي رجع بما أنفق وإلا رجع بقيمة البناء وبه يفتى؛ كذا في قسمة الولوالجية. وإذن الشريك كإذن القاضي فيرجع بما أنفق كما حرره العلامة ابن الشحنة في شرح المنظومة. وإذا قلنا يرجع بقيمة البناء عند عدم الإذن فهل المعتبر قيمته يوم البناء أو وقت الرجوع؟ قولان والصحيح
قوله : (والظاهر الثاني) أراد به ما في جامع الفصولين لذكره بعد كلام الفتح السابق. وقوله ويحمل الأول على ما إذا بنى الخ أراد بالأوّل ما في الفتح من قوله لو هدماه وامتنع أحدهما يجبر» ويخالف هذا الحمل ما قدمه عن الذخيرة من أن سقف السفل وجذوعه وهراديه وبواريه وطينه لصاحب السفل وعليه فلا يجبر صاحب العلو على البناء فيه لأنه لا ضرر لصاحب السفل في تركه بل فيه نفع التخفيف عن سقفه تأمل. ثم ظهر لي عدم المخالفة بين ما في الفتح وبين ما في جامع الفصولين وذلك أن ما في الفتح في الحائط المشترك وما في الجامع في السفل والعلو والفرق أظهر من أن عنها مثلها غير نافذة لا يفتح أهل الأولى فيها باباً بخلاف المستديرة ادعى داراً في يد وقت البناء وهو مبني على أن المبني يبني على ملك الشريك أو على ملك الباني ثم ينتقل منه أيضاً. وفي جامع الفصولين جدار بينهما ولكل منهما حموله فوهى الحائط فأراد أحدهما رفعه ليصلحه وأبى الآخر ينبغي أن يقول مريد الإصلاح للآخر ارفع حمولتك بأسطوانات وعمد ويعلمه أنه يريد رفعه في وقت كذا وأشهد على ذلك فلو فعله وإلا فله رفع الجدار فلو سقط حمولته لم يضمن ا هـ . قوله : (زائغة مستطيلة يتشعب عنها مثلها غير نافذة لا يفتح أهل الأولى فيها باباً بخلاف المستديرة أي سكة كما في المعراج، وفسرها تاج الشريعة بالسكة غير النافذة سميت بذلك لزيغها عن الطريق الأعظم، وفسرها في غاية البيان بالمحلة سميت بها لميلها من طرف إلى طرف من زاغت الشمس إذا مالت وفي التهذيب الزائغة الطريق الذي حاد عن الأعظم، والمستطيلة الطويلة من استصال بمعنى طال . ولم يقيد المؤلف الأولى صريحاً بكونها غير نافذة تبعاً لما في أكثر الكتب وقيدها في الهداية تبعاً للفقيه أبي الليث والتمرتاشي، ويمكن أن يفهم كلام المؤلف عليه لقوله مثلها غير نافذة فجعل الثانية كالأولى بقيد عدم النفاذ . وصورة الطويلة هكذا :

Book of Hawalah, Chapter of Arbitration. The other builds: the owner of the upper floor has the right to its stability, and the owner of the lower floor has the right to repel rain and sun from the lower. So ownership is absolute but the right is a restriction, and they have combined, so we combine them. The completion is in it. And regarding a wall between two: if they have wood upon it and one of them builds, the builder may prevent the other from placing wood until he gives him half the value of the building as built. In al-Aqdiya: a shared wall, one wants to demolish it and the partner refuses: if it is in a condition not feared to collapse, he is not compelled; if it is feared to collapse, according to Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, he is compelled. If they demolish it and one wants to rebuild and the other refuses: if the foundation of the wall is wide enough that he can build a wall in his share after division, the partner is not compelled; if it is not possible, he is compelled. So from Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl, and upon it is the fatwa. The interpretation of compulsion is that if the partner does not agree, he spends on construction and then recovers from the partner half of what he spent. In Shihadat al-Fadli: if they demolish it and one refrains, he is compelled; if it collapses, he is not compelled, but he is prevented from using it until he recovers half of what he spent, if that was done by the order of the judge; if without order, then half the value of the building. So in Fath al-Qadir. In Jami' al-Fusulain: if the owner of the lower demolishes his lower and the owner of the upper his upper, the owner of the lower takes his lower? Since he has caused loss of a right attached to ownership, he guarantees just as if he caused loss of ownership. End quote. Its apparent meaning is that there is no compulsion on the owner of the upper, and what is in Fath al-Qadir is contrary, and the second is apparent. The first is interpreted as when the owner of the lower builds his lower and asks the owner of the upper to build his upper, then he is compelled. If the lower collapses without action of its owner, he is not compelled to build due to lack of transgression; the owner of the upper may build if he wishes, then build his upper upon it, then seek recourse and prevent him from dwelling until he pays him, because he is compelled, like a borrower of a pledge who pays the debt without permission of the pledgor is not a volunteer. If the upper and lower both collapse, the same applies. Then recourse for the value of the building or what he spent: it is said if the owner of the upper is compelled, he recovers from the owner of the lower the value of the lower as built, not what he spent; it is said if he built by judge's order, he recovers what he spent, otherwise he recovers the value of the building, and with this fatwa is given. So in Qismat al-Walwaliya. The permission of the partner is like the permission of the judge, so he recovers what he spent as verified by Allamah Ibn al-Shihnah in Sharh al-Manzumah. If we say he recovers the value of the building when there is no permission, then is the value considered at the time of building or at the time of recourse? Two opinions, and the correct is... His statement: (and the second is apparent) he meant what is in Jami' al-Fusulain because he mentioned it after the previous statement of al-Fath. And his statement: 'and the first is interpreted as when he built, etc.' he meant by the first what is in al-Fath from his statement: 'if they demolish it and one refrains, he is compelled'. This interpretation contradicts what he previously presented from al-Dhakhirah: that the ceiling of the lower, its beams, its rafters, its planks, and its mud belong to the owner of the lower, and accordingly the owner of the upper is not compelled to build therein because there is no harm to the owner of the lower in leaving it; rather there is benefit of lightening his ceiling. Reflect. Then it appeared to me that there is no contradiction between what is in al-Fath and what is in Jami' al-Fusulain, because what is in al-Fath concerns the shared wall, and what is in Jami' concerns the lower and upper, and the difference is more apparent than that... about it similar, not through, the inhabitants of the first may not open a door in it, unlike the circular. He claimed a house in the hand of the time of building, and it is built on the basis that the builder builds on the property of the partner or on the property of the builder then transfers from it as well. In Jami' al-Fusulain: a wall between them, and each has a load on it, so the wall weakened; one wanted to raise it to repair it and the other refused. The one wanting repair should say to the other: raise your load with pillars and supports, and inform him that he wants to raise it at such-and-such time, and have witness on that. If he does so, otherwise he has the right to raise the wall; if his load falls, he does not guarantee. End quote. His statement: (a crooked street, elongated, branching from it a similar one not through, the inhabitants of the first may not open a door in it, unlike the circular) i.e., an alley as in al-Mi'raj; Taj al-Shari'ah interpreted it as a dead-end alley, named so for its deviation from the main road; in Ghayat al-Bayan it is interpreted as a neighborhood, named so for its inclination from side to side, from 'zaghat al-shams' when it inclined. In al-Tahdhib: al-za'ighah is the road that turns away from the main, and al-mustatilah is the long one from istital meaning became long. The author did not explicitly restrict the first to being non-through, following most books; al-Hidayah restricted it following al-Faqih Abu al-Layth and al-Tamartashi, and the author's statement can be understood from his saying 'similar to it, non-through' so he made the second like the first with the restriction of non-through. The shape of the long one is: O (road) O O O O O. Then the one who can open a door in the farthest crooked street is the owner of the house that is in the corner of the second crooked street. We only say he does not have that because opening it is for passage, and the inhabitants of the first crooked street have no right of passage in the second. His statement: (and the author did not explicitly restrict the first to being non-through, etc.) al-Ramli said: the apparent is that the ruling for both is the same, since there is no consideration of the first being through or not through because passage of its inhabitants in the second is absolutely prevented, so the author left it general, including the through and non-through. He restricted the branching one to being non-through because if it were through, the public could pass through it, so opening a door for the inhabitants of the first would not be prevented. The restriction by the author of al-Hidayah following the two jurists happened coincidentally, so many of the investigators depicted it as through and many as non-through. As for the one branching from it, they all agreed on depicting it as non-through. Reflect to understand. End quote. There will come what is in it. His statement: (So the one who can open a door in the farthest crooked street, etc.) what is meant by possibility is conceptual, not permissibility, meaning the one who can conceive of opening a door in the branching crooked street is the house that is in the corner of the branching, because its wall is in it; as for the one before him, he cannot because his wall is in the first. We only interpreted it that way because he is not permitted to open the door in it as mentioned by the author. His statement: (and we only say he does not have that because opening it is for passage, etc.) al-Ramli said: and in Jami' al-Fusulain from Shaykh al-Islam that he has the right to open and pass, then he said: there is difference in reports and difference among shaykhs, and Shaykh al-Islam chose that he has that absolutely, and with this fatwa is given. Then he symbolized (ض) and made it contrary to the apparent narration, and I say: on the apparent narration the texts rely, and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage is a compilation of rulings from the Hanafi school on two distinct topics: (1) rights and obligations regarding shared walls and upper/lower floors, and (2) the rules of opening doors in dead-end streets (za'igha). The text begins with a fundamental principle: the owner of an upper floor has the right to its stability, while the lower floor owner has the right to protect from rain and sun. Both ownership and the right coexist, so the law reconciles them. Then it discusses a shared wall: if one partner builds, he can prevent the other from adding wood until compensated. Regarding demolition of a shared wall, if it is safe, a partner is not forced to rebuild; if it is dangerous, he is forced. If both demolish and one wants to rebuild, he may be forced if the foundation can only support a single wall after division; otherwise not. The 'compulsion' means he can build and recover half the cost from the partner. If a building collapses without fault, no one is forced to rebuild, but the upper owner may build and then reclaim costs from the lower owner, preventing him from using the building until paid. The amount recoverable depends on whether the judge authorized the construction or not. The text then resolves an apparent discrepancy between Fath al-Qadir and Jami' al-Fusulain by distinguishing between a shared wall (where compulsion applies) and separate lower/upper floors (where it does not). The second part discusses the za'igha: a dead-end alley that branches off from a main road. Residents of the first alley cannot open a door into the branching alley because they have no right of passage there. Only the owner of the house at the corner of the branching alley may open a door. There is a difference of opinion regarding whether this is absolute or if the first alley residents can also open doors; the relied-upon fatwa (according to Shaykh al-Islam) allows it absolutely, but the majority of texts follow the apparent narration that prohibits it.

Key Terms
الزائغة (al-za'igha): a crooked or dead-end street that branches off from a main road; also called a 'sikka' or 'mahalla'.
غير نافذة (ghayr nafidha): not through; a street that does not lead to another thoroughfare (dead-end).
المستطيلة (al-mustatila): elongated; a long alley that may be through or non-through.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority view in the passage, who may open a door into a branching dead-end alley?
A) Any resident of the first alley.
B) Only the owner of the house at the corner of the branching alley.
C) Anyone, including non-residents.
D) No one, because the branching alley is private.

Key Takeaway
Residents of a first dead-end alley cannot open a door into a second branching dead-end alley; only the corner house owner may.

Buyu — Sales and Trade
intermediate
12 min
Ruling on Building a Tannur Causing Harm to Neighbors
Intra-Hanafi dispute on preventing building a tannur due to harm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conflicting Hanafi positions on preventing property use due to neighbor harm, apply the principle of istihsan to override qiyas, and identify the mufta bihi (relied-upon) fatwa.

Core Idea
Property rights yield when harm to a neighbor is excessive and unavoidable.

The right to use one's property is not absolute in Hanafi fiqh. When an activity—like building a commercial oven (tannur)—causes excessive harm (darar fahish) that cannot be mitigated, later Hanafi authorities override the strict qiyas (analogy) with istihsan (juristic preference) to protect the neighbor. This lesson unpacks the internal debate among Hanafi scholars and the eventual tarjih (preference) that shapes the fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling (hikm)
Determine what act is being considered and whether the Hanafi position calls it permissible, impermissible, or something else. Note the distinction between commercial and domestic ovens.
The passage distinguishes a large commercial tannur from a small domestic one.
2
State the qiyas (analogical) position
According to strict analogy, the owner has full disposal rights over his property; therefore he cannot be prevented from building anything, even if it causes harm.
Some early Hanafi imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, al-Hasan) held this view.
3
Identify the istihsan (juristic preference) position
Later scholars argued that excessive, unavoidable harm (darar fahish) necessitates prevention. They cited the hadith 'La darar wa la dirar' (no harming nor reciprocating harm) and public interest.
This position distinguishes the tannur from a bathhouse (only dampness) and a small domestic oven.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
The dispute yields a practical difference: under the qiyas position, the owner builds freely; under the istihsan position, the judge can order removal or prevention if harm is evident.
The passage notes that Abu Abdullah al-Saymari gave conflicting fatwas on the same case.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon fatwa)
Later mashayikh, including Burhan al-A'imma, adopted the prevention view when harm is evident (bayyin). This became the operative fatwa, as transmitted by al-Husam al-Shahid and al-Bazzazi.
The old riwaya (transmitted opinion) of non-prevention is marjuh (weak) in later practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وذكر الرازي في كتاب الاستحسان أن الدار إذا كانت مجاورة للدور فأراد صاحبها أن يبني فيها تنوراً للخبز الدائم كما يكون في الدكاكين أو رحى للطحين أو مدقات للقصارين لم يجز لأن ذلك يضر بجيرانه ضرراً فاحشاً لا يمكن التحرز عنه فإنه يأتي منه الدخان الكثير الشديد ورحى الطحن ودق القصارين يوهن البناء بخلاف الحمام فإنه لا يضر إلا بالنداوة ويمكن التحرز عنه بأن يبني حائطاً بينه وبين جاره، وبخلاف التنور الصغير المعتاد في البيوت. هـ. قال الحسام الشهيد : وكان أبو عبد الله الصيمري تارة يفتي بمنع بناء التنور في ملكه للخبز الدائم في وسط البزازين وتارة يفتي بأن له ذلك والقياس أن له ذلك في الكل لكن ترك القياس وأخذ بالاستحسان لأجل المصلحة . واختلف أصحابنا فمنهم من فصل ومنهم من لم يفصل على حسب الحال قال : وكان الشيخ الإمام الأجل برهان الأئمة يفتي بأنه إن كان الضرر بيناً يمنع وبه يفتى؛ هكذا ذكر في كتاب الحيطان للحسام والظاهر أن برهان الأئمة هو والده فقد نقل عنه ذلك البزازي وأن والده كان يفتي به وعليه الفتوى قال : وهذا جواب المشايخ. وجواب الرواية عدم المنع
Al-Razi mentioned in Kitab al-Istihsan: If a house is adjacent to other houses and its owner wants to build in it a tannur for permanent baking as is done in shops, or a mill for grinding, or pestles for fullers, it is not permissible because that causes excessive harm to his neighbors that cannot be avoided, for it produces heavy smoke and the grinding and pounding weaken the building. This is unlike a bathhouse, which only causes dampness and can be avoided by building a wall between him and his neighbor, and unlike the small ordinary tannur in homes. He said: al-Husam al-Shahid said: Abu Abdullah al-Saymari would sometimes give fatwa preventing the building of a tannur in one's property for permanent baking in the midst of cloth merchants, and sometimes he would give fatwa that he may do so. The analogical ruling (qiyas) is that he may do so in all cases, but he abandoned qiyas and adopted istihsan for the sake of public interest. Our companions differed: some made a distinction and some did not, according to the situation. He said: The venerable Shaykh Imam Burhan al-A'imma would give fatwa that if the harm is evident, he is prevented, and this is the basis for fatwa. Thus it is mentioned in Kitab al-Hitan of al-Husam, and it appears that Burhan al-A'imma is his father; al-Bazzazi transmitted that from him and that his father gave fatwa accordingly and it is the fatwa. He said: This is the answer of the mashayikh. The answer of the riwaya is non-prevention.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of building a tannur (oven) for permanent commercial baking in one's property when it causes harm to neighbors. The discussion revolves around the conflict between qiyas (analogical reasoning) and istihsan (juristic preference). According to qiyas, the owner has full right to use his property as he wishes. However, istihsan is applied to prevent harm that is excessive (fahish) and unavoidable. Al-Razi distinguishes between a commercial tannur causing heavy smoke and a small domestic tannur, between a mill and a bathhouse. The bathhouse only causes dampness that can be mitigated. Al-Husam al-Shahid reports the differing fatwas of Abu Abdullah al-Saymari, who fluctuated between prevention and permissibility. Burhan al-A'imma (probably the father of al-Husam) adopts the view that evident harm (darar bayyin) triggers prevention. This becomes the relied-upon fatwa (mufta bihi) according to the mashayikh. However, there is also a riwaya (transmitted opinion) that does not prevent it. The passage classifies the harm as fahish (excessive) and notes that the measurement of harm is subjective; some scholars made a distinction based on the degree of harm.

Key Terms
تنور (tannur): An oven for baking bread, typically large and used for commercial purposes, producing heavy smoke.
ضرر فاحش (darar fahish): Excessive harm that cannot be tolerated or avoided, such as smoke damaging health or vibrations weakening a building.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference abandoning a strict analogy (qiyas) for a more equitable ruling based on utility, custom, or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon fatwa in this passage, when can a person be prevented from building a tannur in his own property?
A) Whenever the neighbor objects
B) If the harm is evident and excessive
C) Never, because one owns his property
D) Only if it is a commercial tannur

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi Hanafi position is that a property owner can be prevented from building a commercial tannur if the harm to neighbors is excessive and unavoidable, based on istihsan overriding qiyas.

Neighbor's Excavation Harming Wall: Early vs. Later Hanafi Positions
Intra-Hanafi dispute on preventing neighbor's harmful use of property

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on whether a neighbor can be compelled to remove a harmful structure and identify the mufta bihi view based on the clarity of harm.

Core Idea
A well harming a neighbor's wall: early Hanafis said no compulsion; later fatwa allows prevention if harm is clear.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether one can be compelled to stop using his property when it harms a neighbor. The early imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, Hasan) held that no prevention is required unless the wall collapses. However, later scholars like al-Nasafi and al-Ramli allowed prevention if the harm is clear (bayan) or excessive (fahish). Understanding this shift is crucial for applying the maxim 'la darar' in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
In the matn, what is the initial ruling for a well/cesspit that seeps into a neighbor's wall? The owner is not compelled to remove it and is not liable unless the wall collapses from the seepage.
This is the starting point from the early narration.
2
Recognize the early Hanafi position
The five imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, Hasan) all agreed: no prevention. The hadith 'la darar' is considered general and specified by other texts (e.g., hudud).
Ibn al-Shuhnah and al-Marghinani in Fath al-Qadir preferred this view.
3
Note the later refinement
Al-Nasafi differentiated between fahish (excessive) harm, which is prevented, and lesser harm. Later scholars like al-Ramli and al-Ghazzi adopted prevention if harm is clear (bayan), based on istihsan.
This shift is a move from literal riwaya to a more harm-preventive approach.
4
Examine the hila example
Abu Hanifa advised a man to dig a cesspit near the neighbor's well to make it impure, rather than forcing the neighbor to remove the well. This shows that the early position sought a stratagem to avoid direct compulsion.
The hila demonstrates that prevention of harm is desirable but not via compulsion.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The later fatwa (mufta bihi) allows prevention if harm is clear (bayan). This is the relied-upon position today, though the early riwaya is still the literal madhhab.
The window case and al-Ramli's commentary confirm that clear harm triggers prevention.

The chapter of al-Hawalah (transfer of debt) and the section of arbitration. A window: the owner of the courtyard is not prevented from blocking it, rather he may build to screen his direction. He answered that he is prevented from that. In al-Mudmarat: If the window is for looking and the courtyard is a sitting place for women, he is prevented, and the fatwa is upon that. I say because the harm is clear. And I say: There is no difference between old and new when the cause is clear harm due to its existence. This is the speech of al-Ramli. He said: (The conclusion is that which... narrated by Ibn Majah in the book of Judgments, chapter 17, al-Muwatta in the book of Judgments, hadith 31, Ahmad in his Musnad.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute on whether a person can be prevented from using his own property if it harms a neighbor. The base matn states that if a well or cesspit seeps into a neighbor's wall, the owner is not compelled to remove it nor liable unless the wall collapses. However, later Hanafi scholars like al-Nasafi differentiate: if the harm is excessive (fahish), prevention is allowed; otherwise not. Imam Zahir al-Din followed the narration (al-riwaya), which is the apparent transmission from the early imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, Zufar, Hasan ibn Ziyad) that no prevention is required. Ibn al-Shuhnah and al-Marghinani in Fath al-Qadir preferred this view, citing a story where Abu Hanifa advised a stratagem (hila) rather than prevention. The hadith 'la darar wa la dirar' is argued to be general and specified by other necessities like punishments. The later dominant opinion, however, allows prevention if harm is clear (bayan), as adopted by many muta'akhkhirin scholars and supported by istihsan. The passage also includes specific cases: throwing debris causing wall collapse, and opening a window overlooking a neighbor's women—here prevention is agreed upon. The commentary from al-Ramli and others confirms that the fatwa today is to prevent when harm is clear, and no distinction between old and new windows.

Key Terms
الضرر البين (al-darar al-bayan): Clear or obvious harm that can be objectively determined.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juridical preference over qiyas for a stronger reason (e.g., custom, necessity).
الحيلة (al-hila): A legal stratagem to avoid harm while remaining within the letter of the law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the early Hanafi imams, if a well seeps into a neighbor's wall, the neighbor can:
A) Compel the well owner to fill it immediately.
B) Claim compensation only if the wall collapses.
C) Destroy the well himself.
D) Get a fatwa for removal.

Key Takeaway
Although early Hanafis did not compel removal of a harmful structure, later fatwa allows prevention when the harm is clear (bayan), and this is relied upon today.

General Rule and Exceptions for Compelling Repair of Shared Property
Rulings on compulsion for shared property repair

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on compelling a partner to repair shared property and identify the conditions that trigger compulsion.

Core Idea
No compulsion to repair your share unless harm is inevitable and property indivisible.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq establishes the foundational principle that a person is generally not forced to repair their own property, even if shared. However, the Hanafi school carves out exceptions when the property is essential and its disrepair causes harm to the partner or the public. Understanding when compulsion applies—and when it does not—is critical for resolving disputes over shared walls, wells, and waterways.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Locate the statement that 'a person is not compelled to repair his own property' (لا جبر على الآبي لأن الإنسان لا يجبر على إصلاح ملكه). Recognize that this is the default: neither partner can force the other to spend on maintenance.
The rule applies to houses, bathhouses, walls, etc.
2
Recognize the exceptions for essential structures
Find the exceptions: shared well (بئر مشترك), waterwheel (دولاب), and screen wall (حائط ساتر) that is crumbling. In these cases, the partner is compelled to repair because the structure has no other benefit except its function, and harm is imminent.
The text cites Tahdhib al-Qalanisi and others for these exceptions.
3
Apply the principle of necessity
Note the condition that triggers compulsion: the property must be indivisible and its disrepair imposes unavoidable harm. If the property can be divided (e.g., a large house), there is no compulsion.
Al-Halwani's principle is introduced in the latter part of the passage but is relevant here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حائطه ولا سبيل إليه إلا بدخوله دار الرجل أو انهدم الحائط فوقع نقضه في داره فأراد أن يدخل ليشيل الطين وغيره فمنعه صاحب الدار أو له مجرى ماء في داره فأراد حفره وإصلاحه ولا يمكن إلا بدخول دار الرجل وهو يمنعه يقال له إما أن تتركه يدخل ويصلح ويفعل أو تفعل بمالك؛ كذا روي عن محمد وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث؛ كذا في فتح القدير .

يأخذ نصف ما أنفق في الجدار ا هـ.
Book of Hawala, Chapter of Tahkim. Regarding his wall: there is no way to it except by entering the man's house, or the wall collapsed and its rubble fell into his house, and he wants to enter to remove the mud etc., but the owner of the house prevents him; or he has a water channel in his house and wants to dig and repair it, which is only possible by entering the man's house, and he prevents him. He is told: either let him enter and repair and do, or you do it for the owner. Thus it is narrated from Muhammad, and the faqih Abu al-Layth adopted it. Thus in Fath al-Qadir. And in Jami' al-Fusulayn from the chapter on walls: if one of them has a beam on it, the other may place a similar one if the wall can bear it; otherwise, the partner is ordered to raise some of the beam etc. As for the second, there is no compulsion on the one who refuses because a person is not compelled to repair his own property, whether it is a house, a bathhouse, or a wall; thus in most books. And in Khizanat al-Akmal from the book of partnership: a bathhouse between them collapsed, and one of them refrained from repair; one of them is not compelled to build with his partner, but his partner may build, then lease it, and take from its rental income for his expenses. Similarly for the diversion of well canals or rivers. But if the canal needs repair such as removing mud and opening blockages and springs, then he is compelled to assist his partner. So there is no compulsion in this issue and its like. And in Tahdhib al-Qalanisi from the book of claims: in a shared well, waterwheel, and the like, the partner is compelled to repair. And in a screen wall with no building on it, if its crumbling becomes apparent, the fatwa is compulsion because it has no benefit other than screening, and that is achieved by building. This is when there is no orphan property or waqf. If there is orphan property, he said in Wasiyya al-Khaniyya: a wall between two young children's houses with a load on it feared to fall, and each has a guardian; one guardian requested repair and the other refused. Sheikh Imam Abu Bakr Muhammad ibn al-Fadl said: the judge should send a trustworthy person to inspect; if he knows that leaving it would cause harm to both, he compels the one who refuses to build with his partner. This is not like the refusal of one of the owners because in that case the one who refused accepted harm to himself, so he is not compelled. Whereas here, the guardian intends to bring harm to the minor, so he is compelled to repair with his partner. I say: waqf should be like orphan property. So if a house is shared between two waqfs and needs repair, and one supervisor wants and the other refuses, he is compelled to repair from the waqf funds, and this has become a matter of fatwa. And when it is known that there is no compulsion on the partner, then the one seeking repair may spend and repair and have recourse if he is compelled by necessity, such as when the shared property cannot be divided, like a small house that cannot be divided, or a bathhouse, or a wall that is not wide. If he is not compelled, like a large house whose courtyard can be divided and he can build in his share, then he has no recourse. Al-Halwani mentioned a principle: everyone who is compelled to act with his partner, if one does without the other's permission, he does not have recourse because he is a volunteer if he could have compelled him, like digging rivers, repairing a defective ship, and ransoming a slave who committed an offense. But if he cannot compel, he is not a volunteer, like the case of the collapse of the upper and lower portions. And similarly, if a partner spends on an animal without his partner's permission, he has no recourse because he could have referred it to the judge to compel him, unlike shared cultivation if he spends on it without permission, for he has recourse because he cannot compel his partner, as in al-Muhit. So he was compelled. And we have mentioned how recourse works, and complete issues of walls will come later in claims and division, God willing. And in the claim of the finder: a wall between two collapsed, and one rebuilt without the other's permission; he is a volunteer if the other had no beams on it. If he had beams on it, he prevents the other from placing beams until he takes half of what he spent on the wall.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the rulings on shared property repair. The first part states that if a person needs to access a neighbor's property to repair his own wall or water channel, and the neighbor prevents, the neighbor is told to either allow entry or do the repair himself. This is attributed to Imam Muhammad. Then it discusses that one is not compelled to repair his own property, but there are exceptions: for a shared well or waterwheel, the partner is compelled to repair. For a mere screen wall showing signs of crumbling, the fatwa is compulsion because the only benefit is screening. However, if one partner is an orphan or waqf, the guardian/trustee is compelled to cooperate to avoid harm. Then it gives a rule from al-Halwani: if one partner is in a situation where he can compel the other (e.g., by going to the judge) but does not, and instead spends without permission, he is a volunteer and cannot recover costs. But if he cannot compel (e.g., because the property is indivisible and essential like a collapsed upper/lower structure), then he is not a volunteer and can recover. Finally, a case of a collapsed wall between two: if one rebuilds without permission and the other had no beams on it, he is a volunteer; if the other had beams, he can prevent placing new beams until half the cost is paid.

Key Terms
جبر (jabr): Legal compulsion by a judge to force a partner to participate in repair or to allow access.
مرمة (marmah): Repair or maintenance of a structure.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is a partner compelled to repair a shared screen wall?
A) Always, because it is shared.
B) Only if the wall has beams on it.
C) When crumbling appears because the only benefit is screening.
D) Never, because a person is not compelled to repair his property.

Key Takeaway
A partner is not compelled to repair shared property unless the structure is essential and harm is imminent.

Special Cases: Orphan/Waqf Property and Al-Halwani's Principle on Recourse
Refinements for orphan/waqf and the volunteer vs. mudtar distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a mutatawwi' (volunteer) and a mudtar (compelled by necessity) in shared property repairs and apply the rule for orphan/waqf property.

Core Idea
Guardians of orphans/waqf are compelled to prevent harm; a partner who cannot compel is mudtar with recourse.

This passage continues with two important refinements. First, when shared property involves orphans or waqf, the guardian or supervisor is compelled to cooperate because they cannot consent to harm on behalf of the beneficiary. Second, al-Halwani's principle provides a key distinction: if you could have compelled your partner through the judge but did not, you are a volunteer (mutatawwi') with no right to reimbursement; if you could not compel, you are mudtar (forced by necessity) and can recover costs. This distinction governs many practical disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the orphan/waqf exception
Find the section starting with 'هذا إذا لم يكن مال يتيم أو وقف'. Notice that for orphan property, the judge compels the guardian even if the other is unwilling, because the guardian cannot accept harm to the minor's interest.
The same rule is extended to waqf by the commentator.
2
Understand al-Halwani's principle
Locate the phrase 'وذكر الحلواني ضابطاً فقال'. This principle states: if a person could have compelled his partner via a judge but acted alone, he is a volunteer and cannot recover costs. If he could not compel (e.g., property is indivisible), he is mudtar and can recover.
Examples given: digging rivers, repairing ship, ransoming slave (where you can compel) vs. collapse of upper/lower portions (where you cannot).
3
Apply the distinction
Test with the examples in the text: spending on a shared animal (can compel → no recourse) vs. spending on shared cultivation (cannot compel → recourse).
Also, the wall with beams: if the other had beams, he must pay half or allow beams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحوالة باب التحكيم
حائطه ولا سبيل إليه إلا بدخوله دار الرجل أو انهدم الحائط فوقع نقضه في داره فأراد أن يدخل ليشيل الطين وغيره فمنعه صاحب الدار أو له مجرى ماء في داره فأراد حفره وإصلاحه ولا يمكن إلا بدخول دار الرجل وهو يمنعه يقال له إما أن تتركه يدخل ويصلح ويفعل أو تفعل بمالك؛ كذا روي عن محمد وبه أخذ الفقيه أبو الليث؛ كذا في فتح القدير .
غلته
وفي جامع الفصولين من فصل الحيطان : لو لأحدهما عليه خشبة فللآخر وضع مثله إن كان الحائط يحتمل وإلا يؤمر شريكه برفع بعض الخشبة إلى آخره. وأما الثاني فلا جبر على الآبي لأن الإنسان لا يجبر على إصلاح ملكه، سواء كانت داراً أو حماماً أو حائطاً؛ هكذا في أكثر الكتب. وفي خزانة الأكمل من كتاب الشركة حمام بينهما انهدم فامتنع أحدهما من المرمة لا يجبر أحدهما على البناء مع شريكه ولكن لشريكه أن يبني ثم يؤجره ويأخذ من نفقته فكذا في تحويل آبار القناة أو أنهار ،آبارها أما لو احتاجت القناة إلى مرمة من رفع طين وفتح سدد وعيون فإنه يجبر على مساعدة شريكه ا هـ . فلا جبر إلى في هذه المسألة ونحوها. وفي تهذيب القلانسي من كتاب الدعوى وفي البئر المشترك والدولاب ونحوه يجبر الشريك على العمارة وفي حائط ساتر لا بناء عليه أن ظهر تفتته يفتى بالجبر لأنه ليس له منفعة تمنعه عنها دون الستر وهو يحصل بالبناء ا.هـ. هذا إذا لم يكن مال يتيم أو وقف فإن كان مال اليتيم فقال في وصايا الخانية جدار بين داري صغيرين عليه حمولة يخاف عليه السقوط ولكل صغير فطلب أحد الوصيين مرمة الجدار فأبى الآخر قال الشيخ الإمام أبو بكر محمد بن الفضل : يبعث القاضي أميناً ينظر فيه ؛ إن علم أن في تركه ضرراً عليهما يجبر الآبي أن يبني صاحبه وليس هذا كإباء أحد المالكين لأن ثَمَّ الآبي رضي بدخول الضرر عليه فلا يجبر، مع أما ههنا فأراد الوصي إدخال الضرر على الصغير فيجبر على أن يرم مع صاحبه ا هـ . قلت : ويجب أن يكون الوقف كمال اليتيم فإذا كانت الدار مشتركة بين وقفين احتاجت إلى المرمة فأراد أحد الناظرين وأبى الآخر يجبر على التعمير من مال الوقف وقد صارت حادثة الفتوى، وإذا علم أنه لا جبر على الشريك فلطالب المرمة الإنفاق والتعمير ويرجع إن كان مضطراً بأن كان المشترك لا يمكن قسمته بأن كانت داراً صغيرة لا يمكن قسمتها أو حماماً أو حائطاً غير عريض، فإن لم يكن مضطراً كالدار الكبيرة التي يمكن قسمة عرصتها والبناء في نصيبه فلا رجوع. وذكر الحلواني ضابطاً فقال : كل من أجبر أن يفعل مع شريكه فإذا فعل أحدهما بغير أمر الآخر لم يرجع لأنه متطوع إن كان يمكنه أن يجبره مثل كري الأنهار وإصلاح السفينة المعيبة وفداء العبد الجاني، وإن لم يجبر لا يكون متطوعاً كمسألة انهدام العلو والسفل ا ذلك ومن لو أنفق الشريك على الدابة بغير إذن شريكه لم يرجع لتمكنه من رفعه إلى القاضي ليجبره بخلاف الزرع المشترك إذا أنفق عليه بلا إذن فإنه . يرجع لأنه لا يجبر شريكه كما في المحيط، فكان مضطراً وقدمنا كيفية الرجوع وسيأتي إن شاء الله تعالى تمام مسائل الحيطان في الدعوى والقسمة ولا حول ولا قوة إلا بالله العلي العظيم. وفي دعوى الملتقط حائط بين اثنين انهدم فبنى أحدهما بغير إذن صاحبه كان متطوعاً إذا لم يكن لصاحبه عليها جذوع ولا له، إن كان له عليها جذوع يمنع صاحبه عن وضع الجذوع حتى يأخذ نصف ما أنفق في الجدار ا هـ.

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq discusses the rulings on shared property repair. Key Terms
متطوع (mutatawwi'): A partner who spends without permission and cannot recover costs because he could have compelled the other.
مضطر (mudtar): A partner forced by necessity to spend who can recover costs because he could not compel the other.

According to al-Halwani's principle, who can recover costs for unauthorised repair?
A) Anyone who repairs a shared property.

The Effect of Denial on Rescission: Sale vs. Marriage
Explains whether rescission occurs by intention or action, and compares sale and marriage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between denial in sale and denial in marriage for rescission, and identify the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani on the effects of denial on inheritance and divorce.

Core Idea
Denial rescinds a sale but not a marriage—the stability of marriage resists dissolution by mere words.

This passage from Bahr al-Raiq delves into a nuanced Hanafi principle: when a party denies a contract or abandons litigation, does that constitute rescission (faskh)? The answer diverges sharply between sale and marriage. Within the Hanafi school itself, there are disagreements among the founding imams on related issues like inheritance and divorce. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the law and appreciating the stability of the marriage contract.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central legal question
What is the effect of denial (inkar) or abandoning litigation on a contract? Specifically, does it automatically effect rescission without explicit action?
2
Separate the issue for sale vs. marriage
Note the explicit ruling: denial in sale causes rescission; denial in marriage does not. Understand the rationale: in sale, the seller's consent is lost when payment fails; in marriage, the contract is more stable and not easily dissolved.
The passage contrasts the admissibility of evidence after denial in marriage vs. sale.
3
Analyze the proofs and conditions
The text states that rescission by mere intention is not sufficient; it must be accompanied by action (e.g., taking possession). Some scholars require verbal testimony as well. Al-Hidayah insists on action. This is relevant to both sale and marriage.
4
Compare positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
On inheritance: Abu Hanifa says a wife does not inherit if the husband denies marriage and dies; the two companions say she does. On divorce: saying 'You are not my wife' with intention effects divorce according to Abu Hanifa, but not according to the two companions.
These differences arise from viewing denial as a lesser act than explicit dissolution.
5
Determine practical consequences (thamarah al-khilaf)
In sale, if a buyer denies, the seller may immediately treat the contract as rescinded and resell. In marriage, denial does not free the wife to remarry; she must seek judicial annulment or the husband must pronounce talaq.
This distinction protects the sanctity of marriage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على ترك الخصومة تم الفسخ بمجرد العزم وإن كان لا يثبت الفسخ فقد اقترن بالفعل وهو إمساك الجارية ونقلها وما يضاهيه ولأنه لما تعذر استيفاء الثمن من المشتري فات رضا البائع فيستقل بفسخه وفي إقرار منية المفتي : رجل أقر أن هذه الدار لذي اليد أنا بعتها بألف درهم ووصل الكلام وأنكر ذو اليد الشراء فأقام المقر البينة أن الدار له تقبل بينته، ولو سكت بعد الإقرار أن الدار لذي اليد ثم أقام البينة أن الدار له لم تقبل، ولو أقام البينة على البيع. منه في المسألتين تقبل بينته لأنه كذلك ادعاه ا.هـ. وبه علم أن الإقرار إذا ذكر له سبب ولم يثبت ذلك السبب فإنه يبطل الإقرار إن كان موصولاً وإلا لا. أشار بحل وطء البائع إلى فسخ البيع فدل على أن للبائع أن يردها على بائعه بعيب قديم لانفساخ البيع. وقيده في النهاية بأن يكون بعد تحليف المشتري إذ لو كان قبله فليس له الرد على بائعه لاحتمال نكول المدعى عليه فاعتبر بيعاً جديداً في حق ثالث وقيده الشارح بأن يكون بعد القبض أما قبله فينبغي أن له الرد مطلقاً لكونه فسخاً من كل وجه في غير العقار إلا بعد حلف فيجب تقييد الكتاب، ودل على أن المشتري لو برهن على الشراء منه لم يقبل واختلف في معنى ترك الخصومة أو العزم عليها فقيل يكتفى بالقلب، وقيل يشهد بلسانه على ما في قلبه ولا يكتفى بالقلب ؛ ذكرهما في المحيط. وفي الهداية لا بد من الاقتران بالفعل بإمساكها ونقلها واستخدامها فإن من له خيار الشرط إذا فسخ بقلبه لا ينفسخ. وفي الاختيار : أنكر البيع ثم ادعاه لا يقبل وفي النكاح يقبل لأن البيع ينفسخ بالإنكار والنكاح لا ألا ترى أنه لو ادعى تزويجاً على ألف فأنكرت ثم أقامت البينة على ألفين قبلت ولا يكون إنكارها تكذيباً للشهود، وفي البيع لا تقبل ويكون تكذيباً للشهود ا هـ . ولو ادعت عليه نكاحاً وحلف عندهما أو لم يحلف عنده لا يحل لها التزوّج بغيره لأن إنكاره لا يكون فسخاً فيحتاج القاضي بعده أن يقول فرقت بينكما أو يقول الخصم إن كانت زوجتي فهي طالق بائن. وقيد بالبيع لأنه لو جحد الزوج النكاح وحلف وعزمت الزوجة على ترك الخصومة لم يكن لها أن تتزوّج والنكاح لا يحتمل الفسخ بسبب من الأسباب؛ كذا في فتح القدير. وقدمنا في النكاح من خيار البلوغ أنه يحتمله في صور بعد التمام. وفي الخلاصة : امرأة ادعت على رجل أنه تزوجها فأنكر الزوج ثم ادعى أنه تزوجها بعد ذلك وأقامت البينة تقبل بخلاف البيع لأن النكاح لا يبطل بجحودهما، ولو ادعى على امرأة أنه تزوجها فأنكرت المرأة ثم مات الزوج فجاءت المرأة تدعي ميراثه لها الميراث كعكسه عندهما، وعند أبي حنيفة لا ميراث له لأنه لا عدة عليه ولذا له أن يتزوج بأختها وأربع سواها اهـ. واعلم أن إنكار النكاح كما لا يكون فسخاً لا يقع به الطلاق وإن نوى بخلاف لست بامرأة فإنه يقع به إن نوى عنده خلافاً لهما كما في طلاق البزازية. وفي البزازية : ادعت الطلاق فأنكر ثم مات لا تملك مطالبة الميراث ا.هـ. فجحود الطلاق لا يرفعه
(Continued from previous) upon abandoning litigation, the rescission is effected by mere intention, although rescission is not proven unless it is accompanied by an action, which is holding the slave girl, transferring her, and similar actions. Because when collecting the price from the buyer becomes impossible, the seller's consent is lost, so he independently rescinds. In Iqrar Munyat al-Mufti: A man acknowledged that this house belongs to the possessor, 'I sold it to him for one thousand dirhams,' and his speech was connected, and the possessor denied the purchase. Then the acknowledging party brought evidence that the house is his – his evidence is accepted. If he remained silent after acknowledging that the house belongs to the possessor, then brought evidence that the house is his, it is not accepted. If he brought evidence of the sale – from him in both issues, his evidence is accepted because he claimed it as such. End quote. From this it is known that if an acknowledgment is mentioned with a cause and that cause is not proven, the acknowledgment is invalidated if it was connected (mawsul), otherwise not. His reference to the permissibility of the seller's intercourse indicates the rescission of the sale, so it indicates that the seller may return it to his seller due to a pre-existing defect because of the rescission. Al-Nihayah restricted it by saying it is after the buyer is sworn; if before, he may not return to his seller due to the possibility of the defendant's refusal to swear, so it is considered a new sale in respect of a third party. The commentator restricted it to after delivery; before delivery, he should have the right to return absolutely because it is a rescission from all aspects, except regarding real estate, except after swearing, so the book must be qualified. It indicates that if the buyer proves the purchase from him, it is not accepted. There is a difference of opinion on the meaning of abandoning litigation or intending it: some say intention in the heart suffices; others say he must testify with his tongue to what is in his heart, and intention alone does not suffice; both views are mentioned in al-Muhit. In al-Hidayah, it must be accompanied by action – holding her, transferring her, using her – because a person with an option to rescind does not rescind by mere intention. In al-Ikhtiyar: If someone denies the sale then claims it, it is not accepted; in marriage it is accepted, because the sale is rescinded by denial but marriage is not. Do you not see that if a woman claims marriage for one thousand and the man denies, then she brings evidence for two thousand, it is accepted and her denial does not constitute refutation of the witnesses; in sale it is not accepted and constitutes refutation of the witnesses. End quote. If a woman claims marriage from him and he swears before the two judges or does not swear, it is not permissible for her to marry another, because his denial does not constitute rescission; so the judge must say 'I separate you' or the opponent says 'If she is my wife, she is divorced with a irrevocable divorce.' It is restricted to sale because if the husband denies the marriage and swears, and the wife intends to abandon litigation, she may not marry, and marriage does not admit rescission by any cause; thus in Fath al-Qadir. And we have presented in the chapter on marriage from the option of puberty that it admits rescission in some cases after completion. In al-Khulasa: A woman claimed against a man that he married her; the husband denied, then claimed that he married her after that, and she brought evidence – it is accepted, unlike sale, because marriage is not invalidated by their denial. If a man claimed against a woman that he married her, and the woman denied, then the husband died, and the woman comes claiming inheritance – she gets inheritance, and its opposite according to them (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad); according to Abu Hanifa, she does not inherit because there is no 'idda upon him, so he may marry her sister and four others besides her. End quote. Know that denial of marriage, as it does not constitute rescission, does not effect divorce even if he intends, unlike saying 'You are not my wife' – it effects divorce if he intends according to him (Abu Hanifa), contrary to them (the two companions), as in Talaq al-Bazzaziyya. In al-Bazzaziyya: A woman claimed divorce and he denied, then he died; she cannot demand inheritance. End quote. So denial of divorce does not nullify it.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the ruling regarding the effect of abandoning litigation on rescission of a sale, and comparisons with marriage. The author begins by stating that if a party abandons litigation (tark al-khusuma), the rescission (faskh) is deemed to have occurred by mere intention, but this intention must be accompanied by an action such as taking possession of the slave girl or transferring her. The rationale is that when the buyer fails to pay, the seller's consent is lost, so he may rescind independently. Then a ruling from Iqrar Munyat al-Mufti is cited: if someone acknowledges that a house belongs to another and states the cause (sale), but the cause is not proven, the acknowledgment is invalid if it was connected (mawsul) to the cause; otherwise not. This illustrates the principle of 'iqrar' with cause. The author then connects this to the seller's right to return the sold item due to a pre-existing defect if the sale is rescinded. The commentator in al-Nihayah restricts this to after the buyer is sworn, while the commentator (the author's own sharh) restricts it to after delivery (qabd); before delivery, the seller may return absolutely except for real estate. There is a dispute on the meaning of 'abandoning litigation': some require only heart intention, others verbal testimony; al-Hidayah requires action. The crucial distinction between sale and marriage is then clarified: denial (inkar) in sale constitutes rescission, so if someone denies a sale and later claims it, his claim is not accepted; but in marriage, denial does not rescind, so a subsequent claim is accepted. This difference is proven by examples of evidence: if a woman claims marriage for one thousand and the man denies, then she brings evidence for two thousand, it is accepted because her denial does not refute the witnesses, unlike in sale. The passage also discusses scenarios of marriage denial and the effect on inheritance and divorce, with differences between Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Key points: Abu Hanifa holds that if a husband denies marriage and dies, the wife does not inherit because there is no 'idda; the two companions say she does inherit. Also, denial of marriage does not effect divorce, except if he says 'You are not my wife' with intention, according to Abu Hanifa. The passage ends with a ruling from al-Bazzaziyya that denial of divorce does not nullify it, so a woman cannot claim inheritance if the husband died after denying her claim.

Key Terms
ترك الخصومة (tark al-khusuma): Abandoning litigation; the act of a party giving up their legal claim or defense.
فسخ (faskh): Rescission; annulment of a contract, often due to breach or option.
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment; a statement by a person against their own interest, admitting a right of another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a seller denies a sale and then later claims it, what is the ruling?
A) His claim is accepted because denial does not rescind the sale.
B) His claim is not accepted because denial rescinds the sale.
C) His claim is accepted only if he brings witnesses.
D) The sale is considered void ab initio.

Key Takeaway
Denial rescinds a sale but not a marriage; this distinction is a core Hanafi principle reflecting the differing stability of the two contracts.

Acknowledgment of Receipt of Dirhams and Claim of Counterfeit
Expansion of a matn ruling on iqrar with conditions and distinctions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on claiming counterfeitness after acknowledging receipt of dirhams and apply the conditions of connectedness and coin type.

Core Idea
A claim of counterfeitness is accepted if the coin type is included in the term 'dirham' and the claim is connected or the type allows it.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq unpacks a deceptively simple matn: 'Whoever acknowledges receipt of ten dirhams, then claims they were counterfeit, is believed.' The ruling hinges on the linguistic breadth of the word 'dirham' in Arabic, which covers both genuine and counterfeit coins. However, the acceptance of the claim is subject to conditions, especially whether the claim is made in the same breath (mawsul) or separately (mufassal), and whether the specific type of counterfeit coin still falls under the term 'dirham'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the initial ruling
The act is an acknowledgment of receipt of dirhams, followed by a claim that they were zuyuf (counterfeit). The matn ruling is that the person is believed. Your task is to understand why and under what conditions this holds.
2
Examine the linguistic basis
The Hanafi position relies on the fact that the word 'dirham' in Arabic is a genus that includes both good (jiyad) and bad (zuyuf, nabahrij) coins. Thus, acknowledging receipt of dirhams does not contradict claiming they were defective; it is like saying 'I received ten apples, but some were rotten.'
3
Distinguish connected vs disconnected claim
The author uses 'thumma' (then) to indicate a disconnected statement. Even so, the ruling applies a fortiori to connected claims. If the claim is connected, it is accepted for all types. If disconnected, it depends on whether the type is considered 'dirham' linguistically.
4
Apply the typology of counterfeit coins
Three types are mentioned: zuyuf (state-rejected), nabahrij (merchant-rejected), and satuqa (base alloy). Zuyuf and nabahrij are still considered dirhams, so a disconnected claim is accepted. Satuqa is not considered a dirham except metaphorically, so a disconnected claim is not accepted; only a connected claim works.
5
Note exceptions and additional conditions
If the person acknowledged receipt of his 'right' or 'price' or 'collection', the claim is not accepted because these terms already imply quality. Similarly, if he acknowledged receipt of the sold item (mabi') and then claimed a defect, the seller's word is taken. Also, if he said 'I received good dirhams', his later claim of counterfeitness is rejected even if connected, because he separated quantity and quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن أقر بقبض عشرة ثم ادعى إنها زيوف صدق لأن اسم الدراهم يقع على الزيوف كما يقع على الجياد والنبهرجة كالزيوف . أطلقه فشمل ما إذا بين موصولاً أو مفصولاً ولكن عبر بـ«ثم ليفيد أن البيان مفصول ليعلم حكم الموصول بالأولى. وقيد بالزيوف للاحتراز عما إذا بين أنها ستوقة فإنه لا يصدق لأن اسم الدارهم لا يقع عليها ولذا لو تجوز بالزيوف والنبهرجة في الصرف والسلم جاز وفي الستوقة لا إن كان مفصولاً، وإن كان موصولاً صدق كما في النهاية فالحاصل أنه موصولاً صحيح في الكل والتفصيل في المفصول. وقيد بإقراره بقبض عشرة لأنه لو أقر أنه قبض حقه أو الثمن أو استوفى لم يصدق للتناقض. وقيد بالدراهم لأن المشتري لو أقر أنه قبض المبيع ثم ادعى عيباً به فالقول لبائعه لأن المبيع متعين فإذا قبضه فقد أقر بأنه استوفى عين حقه دلالة فبدعواه العيب صار متناقضاً. وقيد باقتصاره على قبض الدراهم لأنه لو قال قبضت دراهم جياداً لم يصدق في دعواه الزيوف موصولاً ومفصولاً وفيها : إذا أقر أنه قبض حقه أو الثمن أو استوفى ثم ادعى : كان زيوفاً فإن كان مفصولاً لم يصدق وإلا صدق وهو المراد بما قدمناه. والفرق أن في هذه المسائل الثلاث أقر بقبض القدر والجودة بلفظ واحد فإذا استثنى الجودة كان استثناء البعض من الكل فصح موصولاً كقوله له علي ألف إلا مائة أما إذا أقر بقبض عشرة جياد فقد أقر بكل منهما بلفظ على حدة فإذا قال إلا أنها زيوف فقد استثنى الكل من الكل في حق الجودة وهو باطل كقوله له علي مائة درهم ودينار إلا ديناراً كان باطلاً وإن كان موصولاً؛ كذا في النهاية. والإقرار بقبض رأس المال كالإقرار بقبض حقه كما في البزازية. ولم يذكر المؤلف حكم وزنها عند الإطلاق والدعوى وفي كافي الحاكم : لو أقر بألف درهم عدداً ثم قال وزن خمسة أو ستة وكان الإقرار منه بالكوفة فعليه مائة درهم وزن سبعة فلا يصدق على النقصان إذا لم يبين موصولاً وكذا ،الدنانير، وإن كانوا في بلاد يتعارفون على دراهم معروفة الوزن بينهم صدق ا هـ . والزيوف ما زيفه بيت المال والنبهرجة ما يرده التجار، والستوقة - بفتح السين - ما غلب غشها فليست دراهم إلا مجازاً لأن العبرة للغالب وأطلق في الدراهم المقر بها فشمل ما إذا كانت ديناً من قرض أو ثمن مبيع أو غصباً أو وديعة ـ كما في فتح القدير - ورأس المال كذلك - كما في البزازية - His statement: (And whoever acknowledges receipt of ten dirhams, then claims they were counterfeit (zuyuf), is believed because the name 'dirhams' applies to counterfeit ones just as it applies to good ones, and nabahrij is like zuyuf. He (the author) made it absolute, so it includes whether he explains it in a connected (mawsul) or disconnected (mufassal) manner. But he used 'thumma' (then) to indicate that the explanation is disconnected, so that the ruling of the connected is known a fortiori. He restricted it to 'zuyuf' to exclude the case where he explains they were satuqa (base); then he is not believed because the name dirham does not apply to them. Hence, if he uses zuyuf or nabahrij metaphorically in currency exchange or salam, it is permissible, but not for satuqa if it is disconnected; if connected, he is believed as in al-Nihayah. The conclusion is that it is valid as connected in all cases, with detail in the disconnected. He restricted it to his acknowledgment of receipt of ten (dirhams) because if he acknowledged receipt of his right or the price, or that he collected, he is not believed due to contradiction. He restricted it to dirhams because if the buyer acknowledged receipt of the sold item and then claimed a defect in it, the seller's word is taken because the sold item is specific; when he received it, he acknowledged by implication that he received the very item of his right, so his claim of defect becomes contradictory. He restricted it to his limiting to receipt of dirhams because if he said 'I received good dirhams' he is not believed in his claim that they were counterfeit, whether connected or disconnected. And in it: if he acknowledged that he received his right or the price or collected, then claimed they were counterfeit, then if disconnected he is not believed, otherwise he is believed; that is what we previously meant. The difference is that in these three issues, he acknowledged receipt of quantity and quality in one word; so if he excludes quality, it is an exception of part from the whole, valid when connected, like his statement 'I owe one thousand except one hundred.' But if he acknowledged receipt of ten good dirhams, he has acknowledged each with a separate word; so if he says 'except they were counterfeit,' he has excepted the whole from the whole regarding quality, which is invalid, like his statement 'I owe him one hundred dirhams and a dinar except a dinar' is invalid even if connected; thus in al-Nihayah. Acknowledgment of receipt of capital is like acknowledgment of receipt of his right, as in al-Bazzaziyya. The author did not mention the ruling on its weight upon absolute statement and claim. In Kafi al-Hakim: if he acknowledged one thousand dirhams by count, then said they weigh five or six, and his acknowledgment was in Kufa, then he owes one hundred dirhams at weight seven, and he is not believed regarding the decrease if he did not explain it connectedly; similarly for dinars. If they are in a land where they customarily deal with well-known weight, he is believed. End quote. Zuyuf are what the treasury has rejected, nabahrij are what merchants return, satuqa (with fatha on sin) are those whose impurity dominates, so they are not dirhams except metaphorically because consideration is for the majority. He made absolute regarding the dirhams acknowledged, so it includes whether it is a debt from a loan, sale price, usurpation, or trust – as in Fath al-Qadir – and capital as well – as in al-Bazzaziyya. He restricted it to the claim of the acknowledger because if he acknowledged receipt of specific dirhams, then died, and his heir claimed they were counterfeit, it is not accepted; likewise if he acknowledged a trust, mudaraba, or usurpation, then they are...
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the matn statement: 'Whoever acknowledges receipt of ten dirhams and then claims they were counterfeit (zuyuf) is believed.' The commentator explains that the name 'dirhams' linguistically covers both good and counterfeit coins, so the acknowledgment of receipt of dirhams does not contradict a later claim that they were counterfeit. The key distinction is between a connected (mawsul) and disconnected (mufassal) statement. If the acknowledgment and the claim of counterfeitness are in one continuous sentence (e.g., 'I received ten dirhams and they were counterfeit'), it is accepted. If they are separate (e.g., he first acknowledges receipt, then later claims they were counterfeit), it is only accepted if the term 'dirhams' includes counterfeit, which it does for zuyuf and nabahrij, but not for satuqa (base metal coins that are not considered dirhams). The commentator then restricts various conditions: if he acknowledged receipt of his 'right' or 'price' or 'collection', the later claim is not accepted because it is contradictory; but if he specifically said 'dirhams', it is accepted. Also, if he acknowledged receipt of the sold item (mabi') and then claimed a defect, the seller's word is taken because the buyer's acknowledgment implies he received the specific item as is. The distinction between acknowledging 'ten dirhams' (general) and 'ten good dirhams' (specific) is crucial: in the latter, claiming they were counterfeit is an exception of the whole from the whole regarding quality and is invalid. The commentator also discusses weight: if someone acknowledges a specific count of dirhams, then claims they were of a different weight, it depends on local custom. The types of counterfeit coins are defined: zuyuf (state-rejected), nabahrij (trade-rejected), and satuqa (mostly alloy). The ruling applies to any type of debt or trust (loan, sale price, usurpation, deposit). However, if the acknowledger dies, his heir cannot make this claim. The passage ends with incomplete comment about  trust or usurpation.

Key Terms
زيوف (zuyuf): Counterfeit dirhams that are rejected by the public treasury.
موصول (mawsul): Connected; when the exception or qualification is stated in the same breath as the acknowledgment.
نبهرجة (nabahrij): Dirhams that merchants refuse due to poor quality but still have some silver content.
ستوقة (satuqa): Dirhams whose base metal content dominates; they are not considered dirhams except metaphorically.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) The claim is accepted because it is connected and connected claims are accepted for all types.
C) The claim is accepted only if the person also specifies the type in the future.
D) The acknowledgment becomes void.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts a claim of counterfeitness after acknowledging receipt of dirhams, provided the claim is connected or the coin type falls within the linguistic scope of 'dirham'.
