The Rule of Retraction of Acknowledgment: Common vs. Individual Right
Explains the rule on retraction of acknowledgment based on whether the right belongs to both parties or one

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule for retraction of acknowledgment and distinguish between common and individual rights by applying the conditions.

Core Idea
The right to retract an acknowledgment depends on whether the right belongs to both parties or to one.

In Hanafi fiqh, not all acknowledgments can be retracted freely. The key distinction is whether the right in question is shared between two parties or belongs solely to one. This lesson unpacks that rule and its practical implications for contracts, gifts, and remissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of right
Determine whether the right in the transaction belongs to both parties (contractual, like sale and marriage) or to one party (unilateral, like gift, charity, and acknowledgment).
Common rights involve mutual obligation; individual rights are benefits conferred.
2
Apply the permissibility rule
If the right is common, a denier may retract his denial and affirm, provided the other party has not yet accepted the denial. If the right is individual, retraction is ineffective.
The retraction must occur before the other party affirms the denial.
3
Check timing and acceptance
Once a denial is accepted by the other party, it cannot be retracted. Also, if an acknowledgment or remission is accepted, it becomes final.
Acceptance solidifies the legal position.
4
Consider exceptions for ibra' (remission)
Generally, ibra' can be retracted by rejection, except for two cases: when the debtor says 'you remitted me' and the creditor agrees, and remission by a guarantor. Also, ibra' for sarf and salam requires acceptance to be nullified.
Istihsan overrides qiyas in these exceptions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أن كل شيء يكون الحق لهما جميعاً إذا رجع المنكر إلى التصديق قبل أن يصدقه الآخر على إنكاره فهو جائز كالبيع والنكاح . في وكل شيء يكون الحق فيه لواحد كالهبة والصدقة والإقرار لا ينفعه إقراره بعده؛ كذا القنية . وقيد بكون التصديق بعد الرد لأنه لو قبل الإقرار أولاً ثم رده لم يرتد، وكذا الإبراء عن الدين وهبته لأنه بالقبول قد تم وكذا إذا وقف على رجل فقبله ثم رده لم يرتد وإن رده قبل القبول ارتد كما في الإسعاف. ثم اعلم أن الإبراء يرتد بالرد إلا فيما إذا قال المديون أبرثني فأبرأه فإنه لا يرتد كما في البزازية وكذا إبراء الكفيل لا يرتد بالرد فالمستثنى مسألتان كما أن قولهم إن الإبراء لا يتوقف على القبول يخرج عنه الإبراء عن بدل الصرف والسلم فإنه يتوقف على القبول ليبطلا كما قدمناه في باب السلم ثم اعلم أنه إذا ادعى أنه أقر بالمال الذي أبرأه منه إن قال أبرأني وقبلته لم يصح الإقرار لعدم صحة الرد بعد القبول، وإن لم يقل وقبلته صح الإقرار الجواز رد الإبراء فيبطل فيصح الإقرار وتمامه في جامع الفصولين. وأطلق في الرد فشمل ما إذا قال ليس لي عليك شيء أو قال لك أو قال هي لفلان كما في فتح القدير، والأخير محمول على ما إذا لم يصدقه فلان وإلا فهو تحويل وأشار باتحاد الإقرار إلى أنه لو أقر ثانياً بعد الرد فصدقه الثاني ثبت استحساناً لا قياساً كما في فتح القدير. وفي القنية : لو أنكر المقر الإقرار الثاني وادعاه المقر له وأقام بينة لا تسمع ولا يحلف للتناقض بين هذه ورد الإقرار وعدم علم القاضي بما يدفع التناقض وهو رجوع المقر إلى إقراره. قال أستاذنا : ينبغي القبول وهو الأشبه بالصواب إلى آخر ما فيها من الإقرار. قلت : ويستثنى الإبراء عن بدل الصرف والسلم كما سيذكره المؤلف قريباً.
And the summary: Everything for which the right belongs to both of them, when the denier returns to affirming before the other has affirmed him in his denial, it is permissible, like sale and marriage. And everything for which the right belongs to one, like gift, charity, and acknowledgment, his acknowledgment after that does not benefit him; thus in al-Qunya. And it is conditioned that the affirmation is after the rejection, because if he first accepted the acknowledgment and then rejected it, it does not revert. Likewise, remission from debt and its gift, because it has been completed by acceptance. Similarly, if he made a waqf for a man and he accepted it, then rejected it, it does not revert. But if he rejected before acceptance, it reverts, as in al-Isaf. Then know: remission (ibra) is reverted by rejection, except when the debtor says 'You remitted me' and he remitted him, then it does not revert, as in al-Bazzaziyya. Likewise, the remission of the guarantor does not revert by rejection. So the exceptions are two cases. Just as their statement that remission does not require acceptance, it excludes remission of the price in sarf and salam, because it requires acceptance to be nullified, as we mentioned in the chapter of salam. Then know: when he claims that he acknowledged the property from which he was remitted, if he said 'He remitted me and I accepted it,' his acknowledgment is not valid because rejection after acceptance is invalid. But if he did not say 'and I accepted,' his acknowledgment is valid due to the permissibility of rejecting the remission, so it becomes null and the acknowledgment is valid. Its completion is in Jami' al-Fusulayn. And 'rejection' is unrestricted, so it includes when he said 'I have nothing due to you' or 'yours' or 'it belongs to so-and-so,' as in Fath al-Qadir. The last is interpreted as when he did not affirm him; otherwise it is a transfer. And he indicated the unity of acknowledgment to that if he acknowledges a second time after rejection and the second affirms him, it is established by istihsan not by qiyas, as in Fath al-Qadir. And in al-Qunya: If the acknowledger denies the second acknowledgment and the acknowledged claims it and provides evidence, it is not heard and he is not sworn due to the contradiction between this and the rejection of the acknowledgment and the judge's lack of knowledge of what removes the contradiction, which is the acknowledger's return to his acknowledgment. Our teacher said: Acceptance is appropriate, and it is more resembling correct, to the end of what is in it regarding acknowledgment. I said: And it is excepted the remission of the price in sarf and salam, as the author will mention soon.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a key legal rule regarding the retraction of an acknowledgment (iqrar) or the effect of a denial followed by affirmation. It distinguishes between two types of rights: those that belong to both parties (like in sale and marriage) and those that belong to one party (like gift, charity, and acknowledgment). In the first category, it is permissible for the denier to return and affirm before the other party has accepted the denial. In the second category, once the denial is made and the right is solely for one party, subsequent affirmation is of no benefit. The passage then discusses several subsidiary rulings: (1) The acceptance of an acknowledgment cannot be retracted after acceptance; similarly, remission (ibra') given and accepted cannot be retracted. (2) Remission is generally retractable by rejection, except in two cases: when the debtor says 'you remitted me' and the creditor remits, and the remission of a guarantor. (3) If a person acknowledges a debt and then claims that the creditor remitted him, the validity of his acknowledgment depends on whether he accepted the remission or not. If he accepted, the acknowledgment is invalid; if not, it is valid. (4) The term 'rejection' covers various forms of denial. The passage also notes that a second acknowledgment after rejection, if affirmed, is valid by istihsan. Finally, it cites a ruling from al-Qunya about the contradiction that arises when the acknowledger denies his second acknowledgment, and concludes with the opinion of 'our teacher' that acceptance is more appropriate. The passage is a typical example of Hanafi istihsan reasoning.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Acknowledgment or confession of a right against oneself.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Remission or release from a debt; it generally does not require acceptance but can be rejected.
الرد (al-radd): Rejection or denial; in this context, the act of rejecting an acknowledgment or remission.
التصديق (al-tasdiq): Affirmation or acceptance of an acknowledgment or denial by the other party.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of reasoning that departs from strict qiyas for a more equitable result.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone makes a gift (hiba) and then denies it, what is the effect of his subsequent affirmation?
A) His affirmation is effective and the gift is confirmed.
B) His affirmation is ineffective because the right belongs to one party.
C) It is effective only if done before the recipient accepts the denial.
D) It is effective by istihsan.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of retracting an acknowledgment depends on whether the right belongs to both parties or to one; if common, retraction is allowed before acceptance; if individual, it is ineffective.

Acceptance of Evidence of Payment After Absolute Denial: The Principle of Imkan al-Tawfiq
Ruling with proof – general rule and exception with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi rule that a defendant's evidence of payment or discharge is accepted after an absolute denial, explain the principle of imkan al-tawfiq, and identify the exception when the defendant adds 'I do not know you' along with the intra-Hanafi dispute.

Core Idea
Evidence of payment after denial is accepted because it could be for another debt—unless denial blocks reconciliation entirely.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq discusses a common litigation scenario: the defendant first declares 'you never had anything against me at all,' then later proves he paid or was discharged. How can a court accept both? The Hanafi school uses the principle of imkan al-tawfiq (possibility of reconciliation) to harmonize the statements. However, when the defendant adds 'I do not know you,' the door to reconciliation closes, and the evidence is rejected—except according to al-Quduri.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
The defendant first makes an absolute denial using 'qatt' (at all) or similar, then later brings evidence of payment or discharge. The claimant has already proven the debt.
The denial is emphatic but not necessarily contradictory to later evidence.
2
State the general ruling
The evidence is accepted by the court. This is the default Hanafi position.
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Locate the underlying principle
The principle of imkan al-tawfiq: it is possible that the payment was for a different debt or that the discharge was for a separate claim, so the statements are not necessarily contradictory.
The proof is rational (dalil aqli) — possibility suffices.
4
Recognize the exception
If the defendant adds 'I do not know you' (or similar phrases meaning no prior acquaintance), the evidence is not accepted per the majority view because reconciliation is impossible — two strangers cannot have had a prior transaction.
What qualifies: 'I have not seen you,' 'no dealing,' etc.
5
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute
Al-Quduri holds that even with 'I do not know you' the evidence can be accepted because a person might not know the claimant personally but still have had dealings through an agent (e.g., a veiled woman ordering a representative).
The majority view (including the author) rejects this; the mufta bihi is the majority.

And it is not accepted because reconciliation becomes impossible between his statements, since there cannot be a transaction between two persons without acquaintance. Commentary
This passage elaborates on a specific scenario in litigation: the defendant first makes an absolute denial (e.g., 'you never had anything against me at all'), then the claimant proves the debt, and finally the defendant brings evidence that he has already paid or been discharged. The general rule is that such evidence is accepted because there is a possibility of reconciliation (tawfiq) – for example, the payment might have been for a different debt, but the denial was absolute. However, if the defendant adds 'I do not know you' or similar phrases, the possibility of reconciliation is lost, so his evidence is rejected. The commentator notes a difference of opinion: al-Quduri (from the early Hanafis) accepts it even with 'I do not know you' because a person might not know the claimant personally but still have dealings through agents. The text then discusses various subcases: if the defendant claims payment after a settlement, the timing matters; if he claimed payment before confessing to the debt, it is not accepted due to contradiction. The 'fivefold issue' (المسألة المخمسة) deals with a creditor who claims part payment and the debtor proves payment of a specific amount. Also, the passage covers cases of partnership, claims of retaliation, and the effect of confession by the claimant that the defendant owes a third party. The overall principle is that the court seeks to reconcile contradictory claims where possible, but prevents contradiction when no reconciliation is plausible.

Key Terms
إمكان التوفيق (imkan al-tawfiq): The possibility of reconciling two apparently contradictory statements
قط (qatt): Emphatic negation: 'at all'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, when is a defendant's evidence of payment after an absolute denial NOT accepted?
A) When the defendant says 'I do not know you.'
B) When the defendant uses the word 'qatt' (at all).
C) When the payment was made before the court case.
D) When the claimant denies receiving payment.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts a defendant's evidence of payment after an absolute denial whenever reconciliation is possible, but rejects it when the denial is so emphatic that no plausible reconciliation exists.

Special Cases: The Fivefold Issue and Other Applications of Evidence After Denial
Expansion probe – applying the principle to specific case studies and timing rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the ruling on evidence after denial to the fivefold issue (al-mas'alah al-mukhammasah), distinguish cases where evidence is rejected due to overlapping claims, and analyze the effect of timing and settlement on the acceptance of evidence.

Core Idea
Even after denial, evidence of payment may be rejected if it could be for the same part already received—timing and settlement matter.

The general rule of imkan al-tawfiq has important exceptions and nuances. In the 'fivefold issue' (المسألة المخمسة), a debtor who claims to have paid the remaining balance could have paid for the part already received, so his evidence is rejected. Similarly, if a defendant first confesses to the debt then claims payment in the same session, the contradiction is fatal. This lesson explores these and other case studies from the text, sharpening your ability to apply the principle precisely.

Guided Path
1
Understand the fivefold issue
A creditor claims 200 dirhams and says he received 150, leaving 50. He proves the 200. The debtor then proves he paid 50. The evidence is rejected because that 50 could be the part already received (the 150 includes 50).
The number 'fivefold' refers to five possibilities: the 150 could be from the 200, etc.
2
Apply the same logic to similar cases
If the debtor proves payment of an amount that could be part of what the claimant already confessed to receiving, the reconciliation fails and evidence is rejected.
The key is overlapping claims.
3
Analyze the effect of timing on acceptance
If the defendant claims payment after confessing the debt in the same session, it is not accepted due to contradiction. If in a different session, it is accepted. If he claims payment before confessing, it is not accepted.
Two contradictory statements in one session are inherently contradictory.
4
Consider settlement after denial
If the defendant denies, then settles for something, then later proves payment, his evidence is not heard. But if he first claims payment, then settles, his evidence is accepted.
Settlement implies acknowledgment of liability, so later evidence of prior payment is contradictory.
5
Handle partnership and other claims
If someone claims partnership and later claims a debt from the same person, it is heard because partnership property converts to debt by denial. But the reverse (first claim debt, then partnership) is not heard because a debt cannot become a trust or partnership.
The nature of the claim matters for reconciliation.

Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage elaborates on a specific scenario in litigation: the defendant first makes an absolute denial (e.g., 'you never had anything against me at all'), then the claimant proves the debt, and finally the defendant brings evidence that he has already paid or been discharged. The general rule is that such evidence is accepted because there is a possibility of reconciliation (tawfiq) – for example, the payment might have been for a different debt, but the denial was absolute. However, if the defendant adds 'I do not know you' or similar phrases, the possibility of reconciliation is lost, so his evidence is rejected. The commentator notes a difference of opinion: al-Quduri (from the early Hanafis) accepts it even with 'I do not know you' because a person might not know the claimant personally but still have dealings through agents. The text then discusses various subcases: if the defendant claims payment after a settlement, the timing matters; if he claimed payment before confessing to the debt, it is not accepted due to contradiction. The 'fivefold issue' (المسألة المخمسة) deals with a creditor who claims part payment and the debtor proves payment of a specific amount. Also, the passage covers cases of partnership, claims of retaliation, and the effect of confession by the claimant that the defendant owes a third party. The overall principle is that the court seeks to reconcile contradictory claims where possible, but prevents contradiction when no reconciliation is plausible.

Key Terms
المسألة المخمسة (al-mas'alah al-mukhammasah): The fivefold issue: a specific case where a creditor claims part payment and the debtor proves payment of a portion that overlaps with the unclaimed part

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the fivefold issue, why does the debtor's evidence of paying the remaining 50 fail?
A) Because the debtor did not pay the full amount.
B) Because the 50 could be part of the 150 already received.
C) Because the debtor denied first.
D) Because the claimant's evidence is stronger.

Key Takeaway
Even after denial, evidence of payment may be rejected if it can be explained as part of an already-admitted receipt; timing and specificity are crucial for acceptance.

Buyu — Sales and Trade

When a Seller Denies Sale Then Claims Immunity: An Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on contradictory evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the admissibility of a seller's evidence of immunity after initially denying the sale, and identify which position is mufta bihi.

Core Idea
A seller's claim of immunity after denying sale is rejected due to contradiction, but Abu Yusuf allows it if reconciliation is possible.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq examines a specific scenario: a seller denies selling a slave-girl, the buyer proves the purchase and finds a defect, then the seller attempts to prove that the sale included a clause exempting him from defects. The majority Hanafi position rejects this evidence as contradictory, but a narration from Imam Abu Yusuf accepts it based on a possible reconciliation. Understanding this ikhtilaf is important for knowing how evidentiary consistency is weighed in Hanafi transaction law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and the conflicting claims
The seller first denies the sale ('I never sold her to you'), then after the buyer proves the purchase, the seller claims the contract included a clause of immunity from defects. These two statements are contradictory: the denial of the sale negates the contract, but immunity presupposes it.
Note the temporal sequence: denial precedes the buyer's proof, then the seller brings a new claim.
2
Understand the Zahir al-Riwayah position and its reasoning
The apparent transmission from all three imams (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) rejects the seller's evidence. The reasoning: stipulating immunity changes the nature of the contract from requiring soundness to not requiring it, which necessarily implies the existence of the contract. Since the seller initially denied the contract, his later claim contradicts that denial and therefore cannot be accepted.
The key legal principle: tanaqud (contradiction) in statements prevents acceptance of later inconsistent evidence.
3
Examine Abu Yusuf's alternative reasoning
A narration from Abu Yusuf (reported by al-Khassaf) accepts the evidence because reconciliation is possible: the seller might have sold through an agent who included the immunity clause without the seller's knowledge. Thus the seller's denial of personal sale does not necessarily contradict the existence of a valid sale with immunity.
This reasoning emphasizes the possibility of einmütige (reconciliation) even when statements appear contradictory.
4
Compare with similar cases (returned goods, khul', deposit)
The passage provides analogies: if a seller after denying sale proves he returned the goods, that is accepted because it does not contradict the purchase itself. Similarly, a husband who denied marriage but later claimed khul' is heard because the marriage could have been arranged by his father when he was a minor. In contrast, claims of payment or discharge after denial are generally not accepted due to contradiction.
These comparisons help delineate when tanaqud is fatal to a claim and when it is not.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its practical consequence
The author sides with the Zahir al-Riwayah (majority) position: the seller's evidence is rejected. In practice, this means the seller remains liable for the defect and must compensate the buyer. Under Abu Yusuf's view, if the seller successfully proves immunity, he would not be liable.
The mufta bihi is based on the weight of the majority transmission and the principle that direct contradiction cannot be overlooked without clear evidence of agency.

لما لحق بالعدم يسمع من قوله : (من العدة) لفظ العدة رمز كتاب وما بعده نقل عنه قوله : (وقد أجبنا عنه في حاشيتنا
His saying: (And whoever claims against another that he sold him his slave-girl, and he said 'I never sold her to you,' then he proves the purchase and finds a defect in her, then the seller proves that he is immune from every defect, it is not accepted due to contradiction, because stipulating immunity changes the contract from requiring the attribute of soundness to something else, so it requires the existence of the contract, and he had denied it. Unlike the previous issue about debt, because the invalid may be ruled and discharged from it to repel a false claim. What is in the book is the apparent narration from all. Al-Khassaf narrated a narration from Abu Yusuf that it is accepted because reconciliation is possible: he sold her through his agent and exempted him from the defect. And its parallel is what Abu Yusuf mentioned: if he claimed purchase from a person who denies, and the plaintiff provides evidence of purchase from him, and the denier provides evidence that he had returned the goods, it is accepted due to the possibility of reconciliation. The commentator attributed this branch to him, and affirmed it in Al-Khulasa on the transmission of the madhhab: He claimed against another that he bought this house from him, and he denied the purchase. When the plaintiff provided evidence of purchase, the defendant claimed he had returned it to him, meaning cancellation, this defense is heard. If he did not claim cancellation but claimed payment of the price or discharge, the later scholars differed. Of this type is an incident in Samarqand: a woman claimed against a man that he married her for such and such mahr and demanded the mahr, and the husband denied the marriage entirely. When the woman provided evidence of the marriage, the husband claimed he had divorced her by khul' for the mahr, because it is possible that his father married her to him when he was a minor and he did not know. Of this type is a man who claimed a thousand as a deposit from another, and he denied. When he provided evidence of deposit, the defendant claimed return or destruction: if he first said 'you have nothing against me,' it is heard; if he said 'you never deposited with me,' it is not. He questioned the issue of the book in Jami' al-Fusulayn: it should be accepted by agreement, contrary to Zufar, because he became legally discredited by the plaintiff's evidence, so his denial is considered nonexistent, as in the case of a guarantor: a man proved that he has a thousand against an absent person, and this is his guarantor by order, the guarantor can claim against the absent person even if he denied the guarantee originally, because he became legally discredited in his denial. He said: the distinction is possible: the judgment of payment there is also a judgment of recourse, so there is no need to present evidence again on his guarantee because it was established first. Here, the judgment of purchase is not a judgment of immunity and payment, so the claim must be made, and the contradiction invalidates it, so they differ. And it is possible to respond that his denial does not realize a contradiction because he did not deny the sale and purchase, so the claim should be valid in principle from al-Idda. He denied the sale, the buyer provided evidence, then the seller claimed cancellation: this defense is heard. If he did not claim cancellation but claimed payment of the price or discharge, the later scholars differed. And we have answered in our marginal commentary on it with the summary: the person acknowledging only becomes legally discredited if the judge rules contrary to what is heard due to its being considered nonexistent, from his saying (from al-Idda): the word al-Idda is a symbol for a book, and what follows is transmitted from him, his saying (and we have answered with our marginal commentary).
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding a seller who first denies selling a slave-girl, then after the buyer proves the purchase and finds a defect, the seller produces evidence that he is exempt from all defects. The Zahir al-Riwayah position rejects the seller's evidence because it contradicts his previous denial: if he really sold the slave-girl, he could not have inserted a condition of immunity without acknowledging the sale. However, a narration from Abu Yusuf (reported by al-Khassaf) accepts the seller's evidence, because reconciliation is possible: the seller might have sold through an agent who inserted the immunity clause without the seller's knowledge. This is analogous to a case where a buyer proves purchase from a denying seller, and the seller then proves he returned the goods; that is also accepted. The commentator notes that in Al-Khulasa, the same position is affirmed for a house purchase case. The passage also discusses a case from Samarqand where a husband denied marriage but later claimed khul' to avoid mahr, which is accepted because a father might have married him as a minor. Finally, a distinction is drawn between the contract case (purchase) and the guarantee case: in the guarantee case, the guarantor's denial is overridden by court judgment, but in the purchase case, the seller's later claim contradicts his earlier denial and thus is rejected. The author's marginal commentary on Jami' al-Fusulayn elaborates that the key is whether the judge's ruling is contrary.

Key Terms
البراءة (al-bara'a): Exemption or immunity from liability for defects; a condition in a sale that the seller is not responsible for hidden defects.
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Contradiction between two statements that cannot be reconciled; leads to rejection of evidence.
التوفيق (al-tawfiq): Reconciliation or harmonizing two apparently conflicting statements by showing they can both be true under different circumstances.
الإقالة (al-iqala): Cancellation of a contract by mutual agreement; a form of rescission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Zahir al-Riwayah position, why is the seller's evidence of immunity not accepted after he denied the sale?
A) Because immunity clauses are invalid in sales.
B) Because it contradicts his earlier denial, as immunity requires the contract's existence.
C) Because the buyer's evidence is stronger.
D) Because the seller is not a reliable witness.

Key Takeaway
When a party first denies a contract and later claims a term that only makes sense if the contract existed, the later claim is rejected due to tanaqud unless a plausible reconciliation (such as agency) is established.

Contradictory Claims in Sales: The Rule of Tanaqud
Explaining the ruling on contradictory claims in a sale dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on contradictory claims (tanaqud) in a sale dispute, identify accepted exceptions, and apply the rule to analogous cases.

Core Idea
A seller who denies the sale then claims freedom from defects contradicts himself — his second claim is inadmissible.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a common procedural issue: when a litigant makes contradictory statements in court. The Hanafi school has detailed rules for when such contradictions render evidence inadmissible. Here, the focus is on a seller who first denies the sale entirely, then after the buyer proves it, attempts to claim he is free from defects. The ruling is that this claim is not accepted due to tanaqud, but there are important exceptions and distinctions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core scenario and ruling
State the scenario: seller denies sale, buyer proves it, seller then claims bara'ah (freedom from defects). The ruling: the seller's evidence is not accepted.
This is the direct ruling from the matn of Kanz al-Daqaiq.
2
Understand the reasoning (ta'lil)
Analyze why: the claim of bara'ah implies admission of the sale, contradicting the earlier denial. This is tanaqud, a procedural bar.
The contradiction is between the denial and the necessary implication of the second claim.
3
Examine the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Note the transmission from Abu Yusuf (via al-Khassaf) accepting the evidence if reconciliation is possible (e.g., agent sold). Also Zufar's view that the defendant becomes legally discredited.
The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is the majority view of non-acceptance.
4
Distinguish accepted counterclaims
Claims of iqalah (rescission) or return are accepted because they do not necessarily imply prior admission; they can be reconciled with the denial.
In contrast, bara'ah necessarily implies the existence of the sale.
5
Apply the rule to analogous cases
Compare with the guarantee case (kafalah) where denial is disregarded after proof, and the marriage/khul' case. Understand the distinction: in guarantee, the judgment already covers recourse, so no new contradiction arises.
Also consider the requirement that both statements be before the judge for tanaqud to apply.

قوله : (ومن ادعى على آخر أنه باعه أمته فقال لم أبعها منك قط فبرهن على الشراء فوجد بها عيباً فبرهن البائع أنه بريء إليه من كل عيب لم تقبل للتناقض لأن اشتراط البراءة تغيير للعقد من اقتضاء وصف السلامة إلى غيره فيقتضي وجود العقد، وقد أنكره بخلاف ما مسألة تقدم من الدين لأن الباطل قد يقضى ويبرأ منه دفعاً للدعوى الباطلة، وما في الكتاب هو ظاهر الرواية عن الكل. وحكى الخصاف رواية عن أبي يوسف أنها تقبل لإمكان التوفيق بأن باعها وكيله وأبرأه عن العيب ونظيره ما ذكره أبو يوسف أنه لو ادعى الشراء من شخص وهو منكر فأقام المدعي بينة على الشراء منه فأقام المنكر البينة أنه قد رد المبيع على تقبل لما ذكرناه من إمكان التوفيق؛ هكذا عزا هذا الفرع الشارح إليه، وجزم به في الخلاصة على نقل المذهب فقال : ادعى على آخر أنه اشترى منه هذه الدار فأنكر الشراء فلما أقام المدعي البينة على الشراء ادعى المدعى عليه أنه ردها عليه يعني أقالها يسمع هذا الدفع. ولو لم يدع الإقالة ولكن يدعي إيفاء الثمن أو الإبراء اختلف المتأخرون، ومن هذا الجنس صارت واقعة بسمرقند صورتها ادعت امرأة على رجل أنه تزوجها على كذا في المهر وطالبته بالمهر وأنكر الزوج النكاح أصلاً، فلما أقامت المرأة البينة على النكاح ادعى الزوج أنه خالعها على المهر لأنه يحتمل أنه زوجها منه أبوه وهو صغير وهو لا يعلم. ومن هذا الجنس رجل ادعى على آخر ألفاً وديعة فأنكر فلما أقام البينة على الإيداع ادعى المدعى عليه الرد أو الهلاك إن قال أولاً ليس لك علي شيء يسمع، وإن قال ما أودعتني أصلاً لا : اهـ. واستشكل مسألة الكتاب في جامع الفصولين بأنه ينبغي أن تقبل البينة فيها وفاقاً خلافاً لزفر لأنه صار مكذباً شرعاً ببينة المدعي فلحق إنكاره بالعدم فصار كما في الكفالة أن رجلاً لـ برهن أن له على الغائب ألفاً وهذا كفيله بأمره يرجع الكفيل على الغائب ولو أنكر الكفالة أصلاً لأنه صار مكذباً شرعاً في إنكاره فلحق بالعدم . قال : ويمكن الفرق بأن الحكم بأدائه ثمة حكم بالرجوع أيضاً فلا حاجة إلى إقامة البيئة ثانياً على كفالته لثبوتها أولاً، وهنا الحكم بالشراء ليس بحكم بالبراءة والإيفاء فلا بد من الدعوى فيبطله التناقض فافترقا ويمكن أن يرد بأن إنكاره لا يتحقق التناقض لعدم إنكاره البيع والشراء فينبغي أن تصح الدعوى على أصل من العدة. أنكر البيع فبرهن عليه المشتري فادعى البائع إقالة يسمع هذا الدفع ولو لم يدع الإقالة ولكن ادعى إيفاء الثمن أو الإبراء اختلف المتأخرون ا هـ . وقد أجبنا عنه في حاشيتنا عليه بما حاصله أن المقر إنما يصير مكذباً شرعاً إذا حكم القاضي بما يخالف إقراره، وفي مسألتنا لم يقض القاضي بالبيع حتى تناقض الخصم فلم يكن مكذباً شرعاً كما لا يخفى . وبما قررناه ظهر أن تقييد المؤلف مسألة الكتاب بدعوى الرد بالعيب بعد الإنكار لأصل البيع للاحتراز عن دعوى الإقالة ويحتاج إلى الفرق بينهما كما يحتاج إليه فيما في البزازية : ادعى عليه شراء عبده فأنكر فبرهن عليه فادعى عليه أنه رده عليه بالعيب تسمع لأنه صار مكذباً في إنكار البيع فارتفع التناقض بتكذيب الشرع كما ارتفع بتصديق الخصم ا هـ . جامع الفصولين: ولو قال لا نكاح بيني وبينك فلما برهنت على النكاح برهن هو على الخلع تقبل بينته ولو قال لم يكن بيننا نكاح قط أو قال لم أتزوجها قط والباقي بحاله ينبغي أن يكون هذا ومسألة العيب سواء، وثمة في ظاهر الرواية لا تقبل بينة البراءة عن العيب لأن البراءة عن العيب إقرار بالبيع فكذا الخلع يقتضي سابقة النكاح فيتحقق التناقض ا هـ . ثم اعلم أن التناقض بين الدعوتين لا بد أن يكون عند القاضي يدل عليه ما في الأجناس والصغرى : ادعى محدوداً بشراء أو إرث ثم ادعاه ملكاً مطلقاً لا يسمع إذا كانت الدعوى الأولى عند القاضي، فأما إذا لم تكن عند القاضي فهذا والأول سواء. قال البزازي : وهذا على الرواية التي ذكروا أن التناقض إنما يتحقق إذا كان كلا الدعوتين عند القاضي، فأما اشترط أن يكون الثاني عند القاضي يكفي في تحقق التناقض كون الثاني عند الحاكم. ثم قال في فصل الدفع وفي المحيط : ادعى على آخر عند غير الحاكم بالشراء أو الإرث ثم ادعاه عند الحاكم مطلقاً إن ادعى الشراء من معروف لا تقبل، وإن ادعاه من مجهول ثم المطلق عند الحاكم تقبل دلت المسألة أنه لا يشترط في التناقض كون المتدافعين في مجلس الحكم بل يكتفي بكون الثاني في مجلس الحكم اهـ. وقدمنا أنه المعتمد. ثم اعلم أن المتناقض إذا قال تركت الكلام الأول واستقر على الثاني يقبل منه . قال في البزازية وفي الذخيرة : ادعاه مطلقاً فدفعه عليه
He said: (And whoever claims against another that he sold him his slave-girl, and he [the defendant] said: I did not sell her to you at all, then he [the plaintiff] proved the purchase, and found a defect in her, then the seller proved that he is free from liability for every defect — it is not accepted, due to contradiction; because stipulating freedom from liability changes the contract from requiring the attribute of soundness to something else, thus it necessitates the existence of the contract, which he had denied. Unlike the previous issue of debt, because falsehood may be adjudicated and one may be released from it to repel a false claim. What is in the book is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya) from all. Al-Khassaf reported a transmission from Abu Yusuf that it is accepted, due to the possibility of reconciliation: that his agent sold her and declared him free from defect. And its analogue is what Abu Yusuf mentioned: that if someone claims purchase from a person who denies, and the plaintiff provides evidence of purchase, then the defendant provides evidence that he had returned the sold item — it is accepted for the reason of possible reconciliation we mentioned. Thus the commentator attributed this branch to him, and he affirmed it in al-Khulasah according to the transmitted school, saying: He claimed against another that he bought this house from him, and he denied the purchase; when the plaintiff provided evidence of purchase, the defendant claimed that he had returned it to him — meaning he rescinded — this defense is heard. And if he does not claim rescission but claims payment of the price or release, the later scholars differed. From this genre occurred an incident in Samarqand: a woman claimed against a man that he married her for such-and-such dower and demanded the dower, and the husband denied the marriage entirely; when the woman provided evidence of marriage, the husband claimed that he had divorced her by khul' for the dower, because it is possible that his father married her to him while he was a minor and he did not know. From this genre also: a man claimed a thousand as deposit against another, and he denied; when he provided evidence of deposit, the defendant claimed return or destruction — if he first said 'you have nothing against me,' it is heard; but if he said 'you did not deposit with me at all,' it is not. End. Then the book's issue was questioned in Jami' al-Fusulayn: it would be better that the evidence be accepted unanimously, contrary to Zufar, because he [the defendant] has become legally discredited by the plaintiff's evidence, so his denial is as if nonexistent, thus it becomes like the case of guarantee: a man proves that he has a thousand against an absent person and this is his guarantor by his order — the guarantor can recourse against the absent person even if he denied the guarantee entirely, because he has become legally discredited in his denial, so it is as if nonexistent. He said: A distinction can be made: there, the judgment of payment is also a judgment of recourse, so there is no need to provide evidence again for his guarantee since it was established first; but here, the judgment of purchase is not a judgment of freedom from liability or payment, so the claim is necessary, and contradiction invalidates it, so they differ. And it can be rebutted: his denial does not realize contradiction since he did not deny the sale and purchase, so the claim should be valid according to the principle from al-Iddah? He denied the sale, then the buyer proved it, then the seller claimed rescission — this defense is heard; and if he did not claim rescission but claimed payment of price or release, the later scholars differed. End. And we have answered it in our hashiah with the gist: a person only becomes legally discredited when the judge rules against his admission; in our issue, the judge did not rule on the sale until the litigant contradicted, so he was not legally discredited, as is not hidden. And with our clarification, it appears that the author's restriction of the book's issue to the claim of return due to defect after denial of the original sale is to avoid the claim of rescission, and one needs a distinction between them just as needed in what is in al-Bazzaziyyah: He claimed purchase of his slave, he denied, then he [plaintiff] proved it, then he [defendant] claimed that he returned it due to defect — it is heard because he became legally discredited in denying the purchase, so contradiction is removed by legal discrediting just as it is removed by the opponent's confirmation. End. Jami' al-Fusulayn: If he said 'there is no marriage between us,' then she proved the marriage, and he proved khul', his evidence is accepted; but if he said 'there was never any marriage between us' or 'I never married her,' and the rest remains, this and the defect issue should be equal; and there, in the apparent transmission, evidence of freedom from defect is not accepted because freedom from defect is an admission of the sale; similarly, khul' necessitates prior marriage, so contradiction is realized. End. Then know that the contradiction between two claims must be before the judge, as indicated by what is in al-Ajnas and al-Sughra: If someone claims something as purchased or inherited, then claims it as absolute ownership, it is not heard if the first claim was before the judge; but if it was not before the judge, then this and the first are the same. Al-Bazzazi said: This is according to the transmission that contradiction is realized only when both claims are before the judge; as for the stipulation that the second be before the judge — it suffices for the realization of contradiction that the second be before the judge. Then he said in the chapter on defense, and in al-Muhit: If someone claimed before other than the judge by purchase or inheritance, then claimed before the judge absolutely — if he claimed purchase from a known person, it is not accepted; but if he claimed from an unknown person, then the absolute claim before the judge is accepted. This issue indicates that it is not required for contradiction that both contradictory statements be in the court session; rather, it suffices that the second is in the court session. End. And we have stated that this is the relied-upon view. Then know that if the contradictory person says 'I abandon my first statement and adhere to the second,' it is accepted from him. It is said in al-Bazzaziyyah and al-Dhakhirah: He claimed it absolutely, and he pushed it back on him.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition from Al-Bahr al-Raiq, a sharh of Kanz al-Daqaiq, dealing with the ruling on contradictory claims (تناقض) in a sales dispute. The core ruling is: If a seller denies the sale entirely, and the buyer proves the sale, then the seller claims that he is free from defects (براءة من العيب), his evidence is not accepted because it implies an admission of the sale, contradicting his earlier denial. This is the zahir al-riwaya from Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad. However, al-Khassaf reports a transmission from Abu Yusuf that it is accepted if reconciliation is possible, e.g., if an agent sold and declared freedom from defects. The author then discusses exceptions: claims of rescission (إقالة) or return (رد) may be accepted because they do not necessarily imply prior admission. The incident from Samarqand involving marriage and khul' is analogous. The passage also compares with the case of guarantee (كفالة) where denial is treated as nonexistent after proof. The author resolves the seeming inconsistency by explaining that in the guarantee case, the judgment already includes recourse, so no new contradiction; here the judgment on purchase does not include freedom from defects, so the claim requires a new suit and contradiction invalidates it. The passage also emphasizes that the contradiction must be before the judge; if the first statement was not in court, it does not count. Finally, if the contradictory person retracts his first statement and adheres to the second, it is accepted.

Key Terms
تناقض (tanaqud): Legal contradiction where a party's later claim contradicts his earlier statement, rendering the later claim inadmissible unless the earlier is retracted.
براءة (bara'ah): Freedom from liability; here refers to the seller's claim of being free from liability for defects (براءة من العيب).
إقالة (iqalah): Rescission of a sale by mutual agreement; here distinguished from a claim of defect because it does not necessarily imply prior admission of the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi ruling discussed in this lesson, why is the seller's evidence of freedom from defects not accepted after he first denied the sale?
A) Because the buyer's proof is stronger.
B) Because the claim of freedom from defects implies admission of the sale, contradicting the denial.
C) Because the seller did not pay the price.
D) Because the defect claim is unrelated to the denial.

Key Takeaway
A litigant cannot make a later claim that necessarily implies the existence of a fact he previously denied; such contradictory claims are inadmissible unless the first statement is retracted.

When 'In Shā'a Allāh' Invalidates a Document: Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on exception in documents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether 'in shā'a Allāh' invalidates all clauses in a document or only the last, and apply the distinction to a given document.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, adding 'in shā'a Allāh' to a document can void it — but whether it voids everything or just the last clause depends on which imam you follow.

When a person writes 'in shā'a Allāh' at the end of a document such as a sale deed or acknowledgment, does that exception invalidate the entire document or only the final clause? This question sparked a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and his two foremost students, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani. In this lesson, you will dissect their arguments by qiyas and istihsan, and see how the same principle applies differently to spoken and written statements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core act and ruling
The passage states that if a document (ṣikk) ends with 'in shā'a Allāh', it becomes invalid. This is the general rule.
2
Understand the scope of the dispute
The dispute arises when the document contains multiple items (e.g., sale, lease, acknowledgment) conjoined by wāw.
3
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
He says all items are invalid via qiyas because they are like a single statement due to the conjunction.
Qiyas to spoken statements where exception applies to all.
4
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
They say only the last item is invalid via istihsan, because the exception attaches to what is nearest, and the document's purpose is verification.
Istiḥsān overrides the general qiyas for documents.
5
Extract the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: in a tripartite document, Abu Hanifa voids everything; the Sahibayn void only the last clause.
This difference affects the validity of contracts and acknowledgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ويبطل الصك بأن شاء الله تعالى) أي يبطل مكتوب الشراء أو الإقرار ونحوهما إذا كتب في آخره إن شاء الله تعالى فيبطل البيع ونحوه ولكون الاستثناء مبطلاً. وفي الصحاح : : الصك كتاب فارسي معرب والجمع أصك وصكاك وصكوك اهـ. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا اشتمل على شيء واحد وأشياء وفي الثاني الاختلاف قال الإمام: إذا كتب بيع وإقرار وإجارة وغير ذلك ثم كتب في آخره إن شاء الله تعالى بطل الكل قياساً لأن الكل كشيء واحد بحكم العطف، وبطل الأخير عندهما فقط استحساناً لانصراف الاستثناء إلى ما يليه لأن الصك للاستيثاق، وكذا الأصل في الكلام الاستيثاق وأشار إلى أن الكتابة كالنطق فلا بد فيها اتصال المشيئة، فلو ترك فرجة فإن الاستثناء ينصرف إلى ما يليه اتفافاً كالسكوت . من والحاصل أنهم اتفقوا على أن المشيئة إذا ذكرت بعد جمل متعاطفة بالواو كقوله عبده حر وامرأته طالق وعليه المشي إلى بيت الله الحرام إن شاء الله ينصرف إلى الكل فيبطل الكل، فمشى أبو حنيفة على حكمه، وهما أخرجا صورة كتب الصك من عمومه بعارض اقتضى
His statement: (And the document is invalidated by 'in shā'a Allāh ta‘ālā') i.e., the written document of purchase or acknowledgment and the like is invalidated if 'in shā'a Allāh ta‘ālā' is written at its end, so the sale and the like are invalidated because the exception is invalidating. And in al-Ṣiḥāḥ: al-Ṣikk is a Persian word arabicized, plural aṣukk, ṣikāk, and ṣukūk. He said it generally, so it includes whether it contains one thing or many things. In the latter case, there is a difference of opinion. Imam Abū Ḥanīfah said: If a sale, an acknowledgment, a lease, and other things are written, then 'in shā'a Allāh ta‘ālā' is written at its end, the entire document is invalidated by qiyās because all is like one thing by virtue of conjunction. And only the last is invalidated according to them (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) by istiḥsān because the exception applies to what follows it, because the document is for verification, and similarly the principle in speech is verification. He indicated that writing is like speech, so connectivity in the conditional statement is necessary. If he leaves a space, the exception applies to what follows it by agreement, like silence. The summary: They agreed that if the conditional is mentioned after sentences conjoined by wāw, like his saying 'his slave is free, his wife is divorced, and he owes Hajj to the House of Allāh' if he says 'in shā'a Allāh', it applies to all and invalidates all. So Abū Ḥanīfah followed its ruling, and the two (Abū Yūsuf and Muḥammad) excluded the case of writing a document from its generality due to a deterrent that necessitated...
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of writing 'in shā'a Allāh' in a document. The author explains that if a document (ṣikk) such as a sale deed has this phrase at its end, the document is invalidated. Abu Hanifah holds that the exception invalidates the entire document because all items are connected by waaw. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that only the last item is invalidated because the exception applies to what immediately follows, and the purpose of a document is certainty. They agree that in general speech, an exception after a series conjoined by waaw applies to all, but make an exception for written documents due to the need for clarity.

Key Terms
الصك (al-ṣikk): A written document, such as a deed of sale or acknowledgment, used for record-keeping.
المشيئة (al-mashī'a): The phrase 'in shā'a Allāh' (if God wills) used as a conditional exception.
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): The exception (here, 'in shā'a Allāh') that suspends the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) All three
B) Only the last
C) Only the first
D) None

Key Takeaway
When 'in shā'a Allāh' is written in a document, Abu Hanifa voids all clauses; the Sahibayn void only the last clause, illustrating the tension between qiyas and istihsan in Hanafi jurisprudence.

The Effect of 'In Shā' Allāh' on Written Documents: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Explains the ruling and intra-Hanafi disagreement on the effect of 'in shā' Allāh' in documents

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on the effect of 'in shā' Allāh' in a written document, distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn, and apply the preponderant ruling to a concrete scenario.

Core Idea
In documents, custom overrides qiyas: 'in shā' Allāh' voids only the last clause.

This lesson tackles a key intra-Hanafi dispute: when a written deed ends with 'in shā' Allāh', does the entire document become void or only the last clause? The answer hinges on whether we follow strict qiyas (Abu Hanifa) or custom-based istihsan (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). Understanding this difference reveals how Hanafi jurists balance textual analogy with practical custom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and contested phrase
Define the sakk (written document) and the phrase 'in shā' Allāh'. Understand that the phrase functions as an exception (istithna) that can nullify the preceding statement.
The source specifies that the sakk can contain a single matter or multiple matters.
2
State Imam Abu Hanifa's position and his qiyas reasoning
Abu Hanifa holds that the entire document is void. His reasoning: all clauses are joined by the conjunction 'waw', making them one unit; therefore the exception applies to all.
This is qiyas: treating the conjunctive structure as a single legal act.
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their istihsan reasoning
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that only the last clause is void. Their reasoning: custom ('urf) in documenting transactions dictates that the closing condition refers only to the nearest preceding clause, because the purpose of the document is to secure rights (istithaq).
This is istihsan: preferring custom over strict qiyas.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under Abu Hanifa's view, the entire sale, acknowledgment, or lease becomes void. Under the Sahibayn's view, earlier clauses remain valid, and only the last clause is nullified.
For example, a deed of sale and acknowledgment: if the phrase appears at the end, only the acknowledgment is void according to the Sahibayn.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and its basis
Fath al-Qadir declares the Sahibayn's view as preponderant (rajih) because it is based on istihsan accommodating custom. Thus, the operative ruling is that only the last clause is void.
Later Hanafi fatwa works rely on this view.

Source Text

أصك وصكاك وصكوك اهـ. أطلقه فشمل ما إذا اشتمل على شيء واحد وأشياء وفي الثاني الاختلاف قال الإمام: إذا كتب بيع وإقرار وإجارة وغير ذلك ثم كتب في آخره إن شاء الله تعالى بطل الكل قياساً لأن الكل كشيء واحد بحكم العطف، وبطل الأخير عندهما فقط استحساناً لانصراف الاستثناء إلى ما يليه لأن الصك للاستيثاق، وكذا الأصل في الكلام الاستيثاق وأشار إلى أن الكتابة كالنطق فلا بد فيها اتصال المشيئة، فلو ترك فرجة فإن على قوله ؛ كذا في فتح القدير. فظاهره أن الشرط ينصرف إلى الجميع وإن لم يكن بالمشيئة. وفي وكالة البزازية : وعن الثاني قال امرأة زيد طالق وعبده حر وعليه المشي إلى بيت الله إن يخل هذه الدار فقال زيد نعم كان بكله لأن الجواب يتضمن إعادة ما في السؤال ا هـ . وأما الاستثناء بـ (إلا) وإحدى أخواتها فينصرف إلى الأخير عندنا كما علم في آية رد شهادة المحدود في القذف، وعليه فرع في خزانة المفتين من الإقرار . والحاصل أن الشرط إذا تعقب جملاً متعاطفة متصلاً بها فإنه للكل وأما الاستثناء بـ«إلا» فإلى الأخير، فلو أقر لاثنين بمالين واستثنى شيئاً كان من الأخير، ولو أقر بمالين كمائة وخمسين ديناراً إلا درهماً انصرف He indicated that writing is like speech, so connection of the conditional phrase is necessary. If he left a gap, then the exception refers to what follows by agreement, like silence. Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq explaining the ruling on a document (sakk) that contains the phrase 'in shā' Allāh' at its end. The author first defines sakk as a Persian word for a document, then states the general ruling: such a document is invalidated because the exception (istithnā') nullifies the declaration. The core dispute among the Hanafi imams is presented: Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the phrase nullifies the entire document, reasoning by qiyas that all the clauses (sale, acknowledgment, lease, etc.) are connected by 'and' and form one unit; therefore the exception applies to all. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) rule by istihsan that the exception applies only to the last clause, because the customary practice in writing documents is that the closing condition refers only to the nearest preceding statement, and the purpose of a document is to secure rights (istiythāq). The text notes that both imams agree that in oral statements with conjunctive clauses (like 'his slave is free and his wife is divorced, if Allah wills'), the exception applies to all. However, they differ on the written document due to a custom ('urf) specific to deeds. The commentary in Fath al-Qadir is cited, deeming the opinion of the Sahibayn as preponderant (rājiḥ) because it is based on istihsan that considers custom. The passage then distinguishes the rule for conditions (sharṭ) after conjunctive clauses vs. exceptions with 'illā'. A condition after conjunctive clauses applies to all if it is connected; an exception with 'illā' applies only to the last clause. The author provides several examples from various chapters, including oaths, divorces, and manumission, detailing differences between Muhammad and Abu Yusuf. The discussion extends to agency (wakālah) and the nullification of a document containing a condition, with a note about ibn Abi Layla's differing view on agency without the opponent's consent.

Key Terms
الصك (al-ṣakk): A written document, such as a sale deed or acknowledgment, used for legal evidence.
الاستثناء (al-istithnā'): An exception clause (e.g., 'in shā' Allāh') that nullifies the preceding statement if applied retrospectively.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Preferring a ruling based on custom or equity over qiyas; here used to limit the application of the exception to the last clause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The entire deed is void.
B) Only the last clause of the deed is void.
C) The deed is valid, but the exception is ignored.
D) The deed is valid if both parties consent.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the effect of 'in shā' Allāh' in a written document is determined by custom: the preponderant view holds that it voids only the last clause, not the entire document.

Rulings on Representing the Absent in Inheritance and Debt Claims
Rulings on representation in inheritance and debt claims.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rulings on proof of inheritance when claiming only a share, the effect of judgment on heirs, and the procedure for debt recovery from a present heir.

Core Idea
A judgment against one heir in an inheritance claim can bind all heirs, but absolute ownership claims are separate.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents nine additional rulings (tanbihat) on legal representation in inheritance and debt cases. These rulings clarify when proof of inheritance for some heirs suffices, how a judgment against one heir affects others, and the procedures for debt claims when the deceased has no estate. As you study these cases, pay attention to when a representative is appointed and who is considered a contestant.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between claiming the whole vs. a share
If the claimant claims the entire property as inheritance, proving some heirs suffices for all. But if he claims only his share, the rights of other heirs are not established.
This prevents a partial claim from binding others.
2
Understand the effect of judgment when a possessor claims inheritance
If the possessor (heir) claims the house as his own inheritance, the judgment binds all heirs. However, if another heir claims absolute ownership, it is accepted because the earlier judgment only concerned inheritance.
The basis of the claim matters: inheritance vs. absolute ownership.
3
Representation for a minor heir in debt claims
The judge appoints a representative for a minor heir to hear claims of debt against the deceased. Judgment against this representative binds all heirs.
This ensures the minor's interests are protected.
4
Debt recovery from a present heir's share
If a creditor proves a debt against some heirs and the present heir has a share, the creditor recovers the full debt from that share. The present heir then recovers from the absent heir his share of the debt.
This avoids multiple lawsuits and speeds up recovery.
5
Oath, proof without estate, and the treasury's role
Even if the deceased has no estate, the heir must swear an oath if he denies the debt, and proof is valid. If there is no heir, a representative is appointed for the claimant, but the treasury representative is not a contestant.
The treasury does not automatically become a defendant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث إنما يكفي ثبوت بعض الورثة أن لو ادعى الجميع وقضى به أما لو ادعى حصته فقط وقضى بها فلا يثبت حق الباقين؛ كذا في جامع الفصولين من السابع والعشرين الرابع ادعى بيتاً فقال ذو اليد إنه ملكي ورثته من أبي فلو قضى عليه يظهر على جميع الورثة فليس لأحد أن يدعيه بجهة الإرث إذ صار مورثهم مقضياً عليه فلو ادعاه أحدهم ملكاً مطلقاً تقبل إذ لم يقض عليه في الملك المطلق، فلو ادعاه ذو اليد ملكاً مطلقاً لا إرثاً تصير الورثة مقضياً عليهم فلهم أخذه بدعوى الإرث لكن ليس لذي اليد حصة فيه إذ قضى عليه ا.هـ. الخامس إذا كانت الورثة كباراً غيباً وصغيراً نصب القاضي وكيلاً عن الصغير لسماع دعوى الدين على الميت والقضاء على هذا الوكيل قضاء على جميع الورثة السادس إذا أثبت المدعي دينه على بعض الورثة وفي يده حصة فإنه يستوفي جميع دينه مما في يد الحاضر ثم يرجع الحاضر على الغائب بحصته وهما في خزانة المفتين. السابع يحلف الوارث على الدين إذا أنكره وإن لم يكن للميت تركة وهما في البزازية. الثامن يصح الإثبات على الوارث وإن لم يكن للميت تركة وهما في البزازية. التاسع لو لم يكن للميت وارث فجاء مدع للدين على الميت نصب القاضي وكيلاً للدعوى كما في أدب القضاء للخصاف وظاهره أن وكيل بيت المال ليس بخصم .
Third, it is sufficient to prove some heirs only if he claims the entire property and it is adjudicated; but if he claims only his share and it is adjudicated, the right of the rest is not established; as in Jami' al-Fusulayn from the twenty-seventh. Fourth, he claimed a house, and the possessor said: It is my property; I inherited it from my father. If the case is judged against him, it appears against all the heirs, so no one can claim it by way of inheritance because their ancestor became the person against whom the judgment was made. If one of them claims it as absolute ownership, it is accepted because no judgment was made against him regarding absolute ownership. If the possessor claims it as absolute ownership, not as inheritance, the heirs become against whom the judgment is made, so they may take it by claim of inheritance, but the possessor has no share in it because judgment was against him. End. Fifth, if the heirs are adults absent and a minor, the judge appoints a representative for the minor to hear the claim of debt against the deceased, and the judgment against this representative is a judgment against all the heirs. Sixth, if the plaintiff proves his debt against some heirs, and in his hand there is a share, he recovers his entire debt from what is in the hand of the present, then the present recovers from the absent his share; both are in Khizanat al-Muftin. Seventh, the heir takes an oath regarding the debt if he denies it, even if the deceased has no estate; both are in al-Bazzaziyyah. Eighth, proof is valid against the heir even if the deceased has no estate; both are in al-Bazzaziyyah. Ninth, if the deceased has no heir, and a claimant comes for a debt against the deceased, the judge appoints a representative for the lawsuit as in Adab al-Qada' of al-Khassaf; and its apparent meaning is that the representative of the treasury is not a contestant.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage lists additional rulings (تنبيهات third to ninth) related to representation in arbitration. The third ruling: if someone claims the entire property as inheritance, proving some heirs suffices for all; but if he claims only his share, the rights of other heirs are not established. The fourth ruling: in a scenario where the possessor (heir) claims the house as his own inheritance, the judgment binds all heirs. However, if another heir claims it as absolute ownership (not inheritance), his claim is accepted because the earlier judgment was only about inheritance. The fifth ruling: if there are absent adult heirs and a minor, the judge appoints a representative for the minor, and the judgment binds all heirs. The sixth: a creditor who proves a debt against some heirs can recover the full debt from the present heir's share, and the present heir then recovers from the absent. The seventh: the heir must take an oath if he denies the debt, even if there is no estate. The eighth: proof is valid against the heir even without an estate. The ninth: if there is no heir, the judge appoints a representative for the claimant; but the representative of the treasury (Bait al-Mal) is not considered a contestant. These are all specific rulings in Hanafi fiqh for procedural matters in inheritance and debt claims.

Key Terms
وكيل بيت المال (wakil bayt al-mal): The representative of the public treasury; not considered a contestant in a lawsuit when there is no heir.
خزانة المفتين (khizanat al-muftin): A collection of fatwas; the ruling about recovering debt from the present heir's share is found in this work.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The creditor recovers his full debt from the present heir's share, and the present heir then recovers from the absent.
C) The creditor must wait until all heirs are present.
D) The debt is cancelled if the deceased had no estate.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, representation in inheritance disputes depends on the nature of the claim and the presence of heirs, with specific rules for minors, absentees, and debtors.

Notification of Agency: Only Discernment Required
Ruling on the conditions for valid notification of agency

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the conditions for valid notification of agency and identify why only tamyiz is required.

Core Idea
Agency notification requires only discernment — not justice or adulthood.

When a person is appointed as an agent, he must be informed of his appointment for his actions to be valid. But who can deliver this notification? The Hanafi school takes a liberal view: any person with basic discernment (tamyiz) can inform, regardless of their moral character or age. This lesson unpacks the reasons behind this ruling and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The act is informing the agent (i'lām). The condition for the agent's authority is that he receives valid notification.
Notification is a prerequisite for the agent to act.
2
Examine the qualifications of the informer
The text states that the informer's 'adalah (justice) and adulthood are not required. Only tamyiz is needed.
Tamyiz is the ability to distinguish, typically present by age 7.
3
Understand the basis in fiqh
The reason is that notification is a transaction (mu'amala) with no compulsion (ilzam); it is mere authorization (itlaq). Therefore, strict conditions applicable to testimony (shahada) do not apply.
Agency is a non-binding authorization, not a binding proof.
4
Note the practical effect
If a minor with discernment or a sinful person informs, the agent's actions are valid.
This facilitates commerce by not requiring a just witness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ومن أعلمه بالوكالة صح تصرفه لأنه معاملة لا إلزام فيه وإنما هو إطلاق أطلقه فشمل ما إذا كان المخبر عدلاً أو غير عدل كبيراً أو صغيراً فلا يشترط فيه إلا التمييز .
His saying: (And whoever informs him of the agency, his transaction is valid because it is a transaction with no compulsion in it; rather it is a release/authorization, and it includes whether the informer is just or unjust, adult or child; so only discernment is required.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a ruling regarding the notification of agency. The matn states that if someone informs a person that he has been appointed as an agent, then the agent's subsequent transactions are valid. The commentator explains the reason: agency is a mu'amala (transaction), not an ilzam (binding imposition). It is an authorization (itlaq) from the principal. Therefore, the validity of the notification does not depend on the qualifications of the informer regarding 'adalah (justice) or age. Even if the informer is a fasiq (sinner) or a child, as long as he possesses tamyiz (discernment), the notification is effective. This is because the intent is merely to inform, not to establish a legal claim requiring witnesses. The condition is only that the informer understands what he is saying. This ruling highlights the Hanafi distinction between mu'amalat (transactions) where forms are liberal, and ibadat or shahadat where strict conditions apply.

Key Terms
الوكالة (al-wikālah): Agency, a contract by which one person authorizes another to perform an action on his behalf.
إعلام (i'lām): Notification, informing the agent of his appointment.
تمييز (tamyīz): Discernment, the ability to distinguish between beneficial and harmful actions, reached around age 7.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the minimum requirement for the person who informs someone of his agency?
A) The informer must be a just adult male.
B) The informer must be at least two just witnesses.
C) The informer must possess discernment (tamyiz), regardless of age or justice.
D) The informer must be the principal himself.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, notification of agency requires only that the informer has discernment (tamyiz); justice and adulthood are irrelevant.

Conditions for Proving Removal of an Agent: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on proof of agent removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions required for valid notification of an agent's removal according to Abu Hanifa and explain the difference with the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Removal binds without consent; Abu Hanifa requires proof akin to testimony; Sahibayn treat it as a mere transaction.

In this passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, the author explains when an agent is considered removed from his agency. The key question is what standard of proof is needed for the agent to be bound by the notification. Abu Hanifa and his two companions differ on whether the informer must be just or whether discernment alone suffices. This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the practical consequences, and the later tarjih within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling in the matn
The matn states: 'ولا يثبت عزله إلا بعدل أو مستورين' — removal is not proven except by a just person or two upright persons. This is the position of Abu Hanifa.
The mastur is one whose outward state is upright but inner unknown.
2
State the Sahibayn's position
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree: only tamyiz (discernment) is required, because informing of removal is a transaction (mu'amala) like the agency itself, not a binding imposition.
Tamyiz means the informer is simply a reliable reporter, not necessarily just.
3
Analyze the reasoning (ilzam vs. mu'amala)
Abu Hanifa argues that removal has an element of imposition (ilzam) because it terminates authority without the agent's consent. Therefore, one of the two halves of testimony (number or justice) is required. The Sahibayn focus on the transactional nature.
This is a classical talil (rational argument) based on the nature of the act.
4
Examine the exceptions and conditions
The commentary adds conditions: the informer cannot be the opponent or his messenger; a messenger's report is accepted even if fasiq; if the agent confirms the report, it is accepted; written notification works; involuntary removal (death) takes effect immediately.
These exceptions refine the core rule.
5
Understand the tarjih and analogous cases
The author later states that the correct view (al-sahih) is acceptance of two fasiqs, analogizing to the rule that two fasiq witnesses are accepted in court. This supports the Sahibayn's position for the case of two informers.
The single fasiq case remains disputed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (ولا يثبت عزله إلا بعدل أو مستورين كإخبار السيد بجناية عبده والشفيع والبكر والمسلم الذي لم يهاجر) وهذا عند أبي حنيفة. وقالا : لا يشترط في المخبر بهذا إلا التمييز لكونها معاملة . وله أن فيها إلزاماً من وجه دون وجه فيشترط أحد شطري الشهادة، أما العدد أو العدالة أطلقه وهو مقيد بأن يكون المخبر غير الخصم ورسوله فلا يشترط فيه العدالة حتى لو أخبر الشفيع المشتري بنفسه وجب الطلب إجماعاً والرسول يعمل بخبره وإن كان فاسقاً اتفاقاً صدقه أو كذبه كما ذكر الأسبيجابي، وكذا لو كان الرسول صغيراً. وظاهر ما العمادية أنه لا بد أن يقول له إني رسول يعزلك ويثبت العزل بكتاب الموكل أيضاً، ومقيد أيضاً بما إذا لم يصدقه أما إذا صدقه قبل ولو كان فاسقاً ذكره أيضاً، ومقيد أيضاً بما إذا بلغه العزل إن كان العزل قصدياً، أما إذا كان حكمياً كموت الموكل فإنه يثبت وينعزل قبل العلم . ولم يذكر المصنف اشتراط سائر الشروط في الشاهد وجزم في تنقيح الأصول باشتراط سائر الشروط مع العدد أو العدالة على قول الإمام الأعظم فلا يثبت بخبر المرأة والعبد والصبي وإن وجد العدد أو العدالة وقل من نبه على هذا ثم اعلم أن الإمام محمد بن الحسن نص على خمسة منها ولم يذكر مسألة البكر وإنما قاسها المشايخ وذكر من الخمسة الحجر على المأذون ولم يذكر المؤلف إلحاقاً له بعزل الوكيل فهي ست وزدت عليها ثلاثاً إحداها في الظهيرية من كتاب النكاح قال البيع على الخلاف يريد به إذا قال رجل عدل هذه العين معيبة فأقدم على شرائه كان ذلك رضا بالعيب إن كان المخبر عدلاً وإن كان فاسقاً فلا ا هـ. الثانية في التنقيح فسخ الشركة الثالثة عزل المتولي على الوقف على القول بصحة عزله بلا شرط أو على قول... (والصحيح قبوله وثبوت هذه الأحكام لأن تأثير خبر الفاسقين أقوى من تأثير خبر العدل بدليل أنه لو قضى بشهادة واحد عدل لم ينفذ وبشهادة فاسقين ينفذ، وقوله إلا بعدل» أي بخبر عدل ولا يشترط فيه لفظ الشهادة؛ كذا في فتح القدير
His saying: (And his removal is not proven except by a just (person) or two upright (persons) like the report of the master regarding the crime of his slave, and the preemptor, and the virgin, and the Muslim who did not emigrate) and this is with Abu Hanifa. And they (the two companions) said: only discernment is required in the informer because it is a transaction. And for him (Abu Hanifa) there is imposition from one aspect without another, so one of the two halves of testimony is required: either number or justice. He released it (the condition) and it is restricted to the informer being other than the opponent and his messenger; so justice is not required in him, such that if the preemptor himself informed the buyer, demand is obligatory by consensus, and the messenger acts on his report even if he is a sinner, unanimously, whether he confirms him or denies him, as al-Asbijabi mentioned, and likewise if the messenger is a child. And the apparent (view) of al-Imadiyya is that he must say to him 'I am a messenger removing you', and removal is also proven by the principal's book. And also restricted to when he does not confirm him; but if he confirms him, it is accepted even if he is sinful, as he mentioned. And also restricted to when the removal reaches him if the removal is intentional; but if it is hukmi like the death of the principal, then it is established and he is removed before knowledge. And the author did not mention the requirement of the other conditions in the witness, and he asserted in Tanqih al-Usul the requirement of the other conditions along with number or justice according to the statement of the great Imam, so it is not proven by the report of a woman, a slave, or a boy even if number or justice is found, and few have noted this. Then know that Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan textually stated five of them, and he did not mention the case of the virgin, but the mashayikh analogized it. He mentioned among the five: the interdiction against the authorized (slave), and the author did not mention attaching it to the removal of the agent, so they are six. And I added three to them: one in al-Zahiriyya from the book of Nikah, he said: sale on the difference of opinion, meaning if a just man says 'this item has a defect' and he proceeds to purchase it, that is acceptance of the defect if the informer is just, and if he is sinful then no. The second in al-Tanqih: dissolution of partnership. The third: removal of the mutawalli of the waqf according to the opinion of the validity of his removal without condition or according to the saying... (and the correct is acceptance and establishment of these rulings because the effect of the report of two sinful persons is stronger than the effect of the report of one just person, as evidence by the fact that if a judge passes judgment based on the testimony of a single just person, it is not executed, but based on two sinful persons it is executed. And his saying 'except by a just person' means by the report of a just person, and the wording of testimony is not required; this is in Fath al-Qadir.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage discusses the conditions for proving that an agent has been removed. The matn states that removal is only proven by a 'adl (just person) or two masturin (people of upright apparent conduct). The commentator explains that this is the position of Abu Hanifa. The two Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) disagree and hold that only tamyiz (discernment) is required because notification of removal is a transaction (mu'amala) like agency itself. Abu Hanifa's reasoning is that removal involves an element of imposition (ilzam) because it terminates the agent's authority, so one of the two conditions of testimony (either 'adalah or number) is required. The commentary then expands on exceptions: the informer cannot be the opposing party or his messenger; if the preemptor himself informs, it is accepted; a messenger's report is accepted even if fasiq. Additional conditions are mentioned: the messenger must identify himself, and the report must be written or verbal. If the principal confirms the report, it is accepted even from a fasiq. For involuntary removal (death of principal), it takes effect immediately without notification. The author then adds that Imam Muhammad listed five cases analogous to removal, and the author adds three more. The correct view according to the commentator is that even the report of two fasiqs is accepted, as their combined effect is stronger than a single 'adl, analogizing to the rule that two fasiq witnesses are accepted in qadha. The passage concludes with the condition that the wording of testimony (shahada) is not required; mere report of removal is sufficient. This unit encapsulates a key difference within the Hanafi school regarding the evidentiary standard for revocation of agency.

Key Terms
عدل ('adl): A just person who avoids major sins and persists in minor ones; his testimony is accepted.
مستور (mastūr): A person whose outward appearance is upright but whose inner state is unknown; two such persons suffice for notification of removal.
إلزام (ilzām): Imposition, binding obligation; removal of an agent has an element of ilzam because it terminates authority without the agent's consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa (as stated in the passage), how is the removal of an agent proven?
A) By any informer with discernment
B) By one just person or two upright persons
C) Only by written document
D) Only by the agent's own admission

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school's disagreement on the proof standard for removal of an agent turns on whether removal is classified as a binding imposition (ilzam) or a mere transaction (mu'amala).
